In the Rue Saint-Denis, near the Rue du Petit-Lion, an old house represented historic Paris with its weathered walls and triangular roof. On a rainy March morning, a young man in a cloak studied this sixteenth-century structure, captivated by its modest windows that hinted at early French carpentry. Below, the shop "Cat and Racket" displayed a wooden beam with faded letters reading “Guillaume, Successor to Master Chevrel,” showcasing merchants' creative marketing. Dressed in fine clothes, he contrasted with the muddy street. The still atmosphere was broken by distant market sounds, and his intense gaze was interrupted when three apprentices playfully doused him with shaving cream. A beautiful girl appeared at the window but retreated, leaving him feeling as if a bright star had vanished. Meanwhile, shopkeeper Monsieur Guillaume prepared to open his reputable establishment, known for integrity and profitable connections.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Monsieur Guillaume, an old merchant, stood at his shop's threshold on Rue Saint-Denis, reflecting nostalgically on the past. His traditional attire and gray hair symbolized his old-fashioned values and years of experience in commerce. A staunch traditionalist, he lamented the loss of customs and maintained strict discipline over his three apprentices, who feared his scrutiny. Their relief came when a suspicious stranger left the shop, easing their worries about a prank they had played.Guillaume emphasized a strong work ethic, contrasting sharply with modern shops. Despite their wealthy backgrounds, the apprentices worked diligently under his authority, which stressed hard work and moral integrity. The shop was a model of cleanliness, reflecting their disciplined lifestyle. Although the apprentices paid for their board, they were not allowed to join family meals beyond the basics and accompanied the Guillaume family to church every Sunday.The eldest apprentice, Joseph Lebas, aspired to marry Virginie, Guillaume's elder daughter, but secretly loved Augustine, the younger daughter. This tension highlighted the rigid social structure within the household, where respect and tradition dictated relationships. Virginie, resembling her austere mother, was patient and gentle, while eighteen-year-old Augustine was delicate and full of youthful candor. Their upbringing focused on practical skills and moral education, leaving little room for personal aspirations.Augustine occasionally yearned for excitement, igniting secret romances that stirred feelings in Lebas, who admired her from afar. Virginie also found herself drawn to him, illustrating the complexities of love within tradition. Guillaume worried his daughters might struggle to find suitable suitors, reflecting the challenges of navigating love in their rigid lives.Months earlier, a young painter, recently returned from Rome, paused outside the darkened shop, captivated by a scene of the family at dinner, particularly Augustine. Overwhelmed, he spent months painting, neglecting friends. When his friend Girodet visited, he recognized the depth of emotion in the portraits, which later caused a sensation at an exhibition.Meanwhile, Augustine learned about the exhibition from Madame Roguin. Visiting the Louvre, she was struck by her own portrait and felt a connection to the artist, who had been watching her. Their eyes met, igniting feelings of love and excitement. However, she felt guilty, caught in conflicting emotions. Back home, her family’s mundane concerns contrasted sharply with her newfound feelings, leaving her confused.That night, Augustine reflected on her experience, dreaming of a future with the artist. The next day, her parents returned from the Salon disappointed, having found the paintings removed. Yet, Augustine sensed a deeper significance in the absence of her portrait, hinting at the delicate emotions beginning to blossom within her. Thus, the story unfolds, revealing the intertwined lives of the characters and the transformative power of love and art amidst their constraints.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################The morning after a ball, painter Theodore de Sommervieux eagerly awaited a meeting with his love, Augustine, who was under the watchful eyes of her mother, Madame Guillaume, and Mademoiselle Virginie. Frustrated by rivals and strict supervision, Theodore bribed a maid to facilitate secret exchanges, leading to clandestine meetings during Mass at Saint-Leu.Meanwhile, the Cat and Racket household was busy with stock-taking, allowing Augustine to escape scrutiny. After weeks of labor, the stock-taking concluded, and the family celebrated. The next morning, Monsieur Guillaume resumed his routine, reflecting on his business burdens.In a pivotal moment, Guillaume offered his assistant, Joseph Lebas, a partnership, recognizing his loyalty. Joseph, who had feelings for Augustine, was initially overwhelmed but soon learned of her affection for Theodore. This revelation led to a tense confrontation between Guillaume and Joseph, as Guillaume insisted that Augustine could not marry before Virginie.As tensions rose, Augustine bravely confessed her love for Theodore to her parents, asserting that marrying anyone else would make her miserable. Her mother expressed disdain for artists, while Guillaume, though initially resistant, began to soften upon hearing of Theodore's respectable background.The arrival of Madame Roguin, a family friend, shifted the dynamics. She praised Theodore, revealing his recent honors and wealth, which piqued the interest of both Guillaume and Madame Guillaume. Ultimately, Guillaume agreed to the marriage, albeit with conditions regarding financial security to protect Augustine's fortune. The evening concluded with a celebration, as the lovers found happiness amidst their families' expectations.A few months later, the high altar of Saint-Leu hosted two contrasting weddings: Augustine and Theodore, radiant in love, and Virginie, dressed simply. Monsieur Guillaume wanted Virginie to marry first, but the priests favored Augustine. Neighbors praised Virginie for her practical choice, while others envied Augustine's marriage to an artist. After a grand celebration, the newlyweds settled into their home, where Theodore's passion made their first year blissful.As time passed, Theodore felt the need to return to work, especially with Augustine expecting their first child. He began socializing again, particularly at the Duchesse de Carigliano's gatherings. Augustine enjoyed the social scene but soon realized her lack of sophistication compared to her husband. Theodore's artistic nature craved deeper intellectual connection, which Augustine struggled to provide. Her simple expressions of admiration for his work began to frustrate him, revealing a growing disconnect.Despite her efforts to adapt, Augustine remained unaware of the artistic world, leading to Theodore's dissatisfaction. He sought solace in his studio, hoping exposure to artists would inspire her. However, Augustine's discomfort in artistic circles only widened the gap between them. As Theodore's interest in the Duchesse grew, Augustine felt abandoned, grappling with feelings of inadequacy.Three years into their marriage, Augustine, once vibrant, became consumed by grief. She attempted to fulfill her duties as a wife, hoping to rekindle Theodore's affection, but her efforts went unrecognized. Their initial happiness faded, overshadowed by misunderstandings and unspoken resentments. Augustine's attempts to improve herself through education met with limited success, as her upbringing hindered her growth.Feeling increasingly isolated, Augustine sought comfort from her family, returning to her childhood home. There, she confided in her sister Virginie and brother-in-law Joseph Lebas, who offered practical advice about her marriage. However, Augustine left feeling conflicted, unsure of how to navigate her troubled relationship with Theodore.Desperate for guidance, Augustine sought out the Duchesse de Carigliano, whom she believed had captured her husband's heart. Struck by the duchess's luxurious surroundings, Augustine felt overwhelmed when the duchess offered advice on love and marriage, suggesting women maintain an air of mystery with men of genius. Augustine grappled with the idea of manipulating her husband to regain his affection.After their conversation, Augustine returned home filled with hope and anxiety, preparing to confront Theodore using a portrait he painted of her to rekindle their connection. However, when he arrived, his reaction was one of anger and jealousy, leading to a tumultuous confrontation where he destroyed the portrait, symbolizing their unraveling relationship.The next morning, Madame Guillaume found Augustine in despair, surrounded by the remnants of the shattered portrait. Augustine's emotional state reflected the toll her marriage had taken on her, and her mother misinterpreted her pain as mere disappointment in her husband.Ultimately, Augustine's struggles culminated in tragedy as she succumbed to the pressures of her tumultuous life. Her epitaph serves as a poignant reminder of the challenges faced by those who love deeply but cannot endure the complexities of passion and genius.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################The Comte de Fontaine, a loyal royalist from Poitou, supported the Bourbon cause during the French Revolution but faced financial ruin when his estate was confiscated. He married Mademoiselle de Kergarouet from a noble yet impoverished Breton family. After the second revolution, he moved to Paris with his large family, seeking royal favor. Initially promoted to brigadier-general, he soon felt unrecognized by the monarchy.Disillusioned by former comrades pursuing wealth, he considered returning to his estate. However, after March 20, 1815, he renewed his support for the monarchy, becoming a loyal servant to Louis XVIII during the king's brief exile. He secured various positions, including a seat on the Privy Council, which helped his sons advance in civil service and military roles.Despite the family's newfound status, the Comte struggled with the marriage prospects of his three daughters, particularly the youngest, Emilie. While his elder daughters married well, Emilie's high standards and spoiled nature made her difficult to please. She enjoyed Parisian luxuries and was admired for her beauty but remained indifferent to her suitors.Emilie’s upbringing fostered a sense of superiority, leading her to dismiss potential husbands over trivial flaws. Her father's eagerness for a suitable match faced challenges as her haughty demeanor alienated many suitors. The Comte de Kergarouet, a vice-admiral, expressed surprise at meeting the son or grandson of a friend and apologized for his earlier behavior, attributing it to too much drink. He invited Monsieur Longueville to dinner at the Villa Planat, promising introductions to five beautiful women in Paris.Despite initial coldness, Longueville was charmed by the Count's cordiality and accepted the invitation. During their ride, the Count showcased his marksmanship, and Longueville matched his skill. Curious about Longueville's character, the Count inquired about debts and mistresses, to which Longueville replied negatively, prompting the Count to lament the changing morals of youth.At the Villa Planat, Longueville impressed the family with his elegance and knowledge. He declined dinner, citing his sister's health, which intrigued them further. Emilie was particularly captivated by him, though she initially concealed her feelings. As Longueville visited more frequently, Emilie's affection grew, and her family noticed her change in demeanor.Emilie became increasingly drawn to Longueville, who remained mysterious about his background. She masked her shock with sarcasm but felt betrayed and angry. This revelation led to emotional turmoil, and despite her family's hopes for her recovery, Emilie returned to her old ways, criticizing those in trade and clinging to her noble ideals.As time passed, Emilie attempted to move on, but Longueville's memory lingered. She met his brother, Auguste, who spoke highly of Maximilien's character, complicating her feelings further. Bartolomeo expressed despair over the Bonaparte brothers' lack of support, feeling they had forgotten their Corsican roots. Napoleon explained he could not protect Bartolomeo but offered him a chance to live in Paris, away from vendetta culture.Bartolomeo accepted, pledging loyalty to the Bonaparte family and expressing gratitude for their recognition of his plight. Napoleon promised financial assistance, emphasizing the importance of adhering to the law in Paris, where personal justice was not tolerated. Fifteen years later, Bartolomeo's life in Paris set the stage for future events intertwined with themes of vengeance and familial loyalty.Servin, a distinguished artist, established a studio for young girls interested in painting. At around forty, he was dedicated to his art and married to a general's daughter. Initially, mothers brought their daughters to the studio, but soon they felt comfortable sending them alone, reassured by Servin's integrity and quality instruction.To maintain a harmonious class composition, Servin only accepted pupils from wealthy families, even refusing aspiring professional artists. His careful approach earned him a stellar reputation, making him synonymous with feminine art education.The studio was meticulously arranged, with a walled-off entrance leading to a spacious attic filled with light from large glass panes. The walls were adorned with caricatures and models, creating an atmosphere that blended artistic chaos with elegance. On a bright July day, the studio buzzed with activity as young girls painted, laughed, and formed distinct cliques based on social status.Among them was Ginevra Piombo, the most beautiful and modest girl, who sat apart from her peers. The aristocratic group, led by Amelie Thirion, often looked down on the wealthier but less refined girls from the banking sector. He recoiled upon seeing Ginevra, but Servin introduced her as the daughter of a loyal friend of the Emperor. Luigi expressed despair over his cowardice during Labedoyere's execution, and Ginevra offered her savings, inspiring hope in him.As they spoke, Ginevra felt a deep connection to Luigi, and their bond grew. Servin asked if Luigi could save Labedoyere, but he could only seek revenge. The next day, Luigi shared his harrowing experiences from the retreats at Moscow and Waterloo, captivating Ginevra. Ginevra reassured her mother, and Piombo's joy was evident as he rubbed his hands, a habit from his court days. “Now, to dinner!” he called, referring to Ginevra as “Signora Piombellina.” During dinner, he noted her long hours at the studio, suggesting painting had become more important than family. Ginevra, preoccupied, eventually confessed, “I love a young man.”Her parents were taken aback. When asked about him, she revealed he was without fortune and a comrade of Labedoyere, who had been proscribed. Piombo reacted with disbelief, insisting that Ginevra should love no one but him. She countered that love was not a choice.“I thought you would remain faithful to me,” Piombo lamented, but Ginevra reminded him of his own absences for political missions. “Life has necessities,” she defended her feelings.Piombo’s anger flared at the thought of losing his daughter. Ginevra pleaded for understanding, promising her love would not diminish her affection for him. “You will learn to know the man to whom you bequeath me,” she insisted.Piombo could not accept this. “If he loved you as you deserve, he would kill me,” he declared, his rage palpable. Ginevra maintained that her love was pure and sincere.The tension escalated, with Piombo declaring he would never allow the marriage. Ginevra stood firm, stating, “I am Ginevra Piombo, and I declare that within six months I shall be the wife of Luigi Porta.” Piombo’s fury intensified, warning her of the consequences of marrying a Porta, the family responsible for her brother’s death. Ginevra argued that Luigi was innocent, having been just a child during the vendetta.Despite her father’s threats, Ginevra remained resolute. “I will be his wife,” she asserted, leading to a confrontation where Piombo nearly attacked her but ultimately hesitated.The next day, Ginevra found herself barred from leaving the house. She communicated with Luigi through a chambermaid, and their love deepened despite the growing rift with her father.On Ginevra’s birthday, her mother attempted to mediate, but the arrival of two notaries to discuss her marriage to Luigi escalated tensions. Piombo confronted the notaries, who were merely doing their duty.In desperation, Ginevra knelt before her father, pleading for his consent. “Give me Luigi, or I die!” she cried, but Piombo, overwhelmed, rejected her. “You have no father,” he declared, casting her out.Ginevra entered Luigi’s modest lodging, declaring, “My father and my mother have cast me off.” Luigi promised to love her in place of her parents, and Ginevra resolved to find happiness with him. “Then let us be happy,” she said, her spirit unbroken.The day after Ginevra was expelled from her father’s house, she sought refuge with Madame Servin, who coldly refused to help. Ginevra began copying paintings, quickly gaining clients, while Luigi struggled until he decided to use his handwriting for copying services. His efforts paid off, allowing them to support themselves comfortably.This period marked the happiest time in their lives, filled with love and shared labor. This masterpiece symbolized their happiness and commitment to one another. Another year passed, and their life together continued in blissful harmony, marked by their enduring love.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################In early 1819, Ginevra's picture-dealers encouraged her to create original works due to rising competition. In despair, Luigi sold Ginevra’s remaining paintings and furniture, but their situation continued to deteriorate. She expressed her love and regret before succumbing to exhaustion in Luigi's arms. When he learns of Madame Firmiani, he offers his approval, but Octave admits he has indeed ruined himself for her.Monsieur de Bourbonne is skeptical, recalling how women have historically led men astray. Octave defends Madame Firmiani, presenting a letter from her that reveals the depth of their relationship. The letter expresses her love but also her disappointment in Octave for not addressing the dishonor associated with his inheritance, received through his father's wrongful actions.As Octave reads the letter, it becomes clear that Madame Firmiani values integrity and respect above all. She urges him to consider the moral implications of his wealth and to act justly, regardless of their love. This revelation deeply affects Octave, prompting him to confront his past and seek restitution for his father's misdeeds.He explains to his uncle that he has begun to make amends, using his own resources to repay the Bourgneufs, a family wronged by his father. Octave describes the emotional experience of returning their lost fortune, which brings him a sense of redemption and happiness that far surpasses material wealth.Monsieur de Bourbonne, moved by Octave's transformation, offers to help financially, but Octave insists on earning his own way. He expresses his desire to build a future with Madame Firmiani, who has inspired him to embrace honesty and integrity.Just then, Madame Firmiani arrives with news of her late husband's death and his will, which grants her a fortune. She encourages Octave to accept her wealth, emphasizing that their love is the true treasure. The story concludes with Monsieur de Bourbonne reflecting on love and the virtues of the younger generation, recognizing the depth of their bond.In summary, the narrative explores themes of love, integrity, and redemption, highlighting the complexities of relationships and the importance of moral values in personal connections.######################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################The Rue du Tourniquet-Saint-Jean, a narrow street near the Hotel de Ville, ended at Rue Martroi, once marked by a turnstile until 1823. Less than six feet wide, it often flooded and received little sunlight.Many old Paris streets resembled this gloomy labyrinth, where historical curiosities remained. A notable house at the corner had iron rings on its walls, remnants of chains for public safety. This ancient structure featured elevated cellars and a door accessed by three steps, with high, barred windows.Inside, an old woman tended a fire to cook a stew in a sparsely furnished but clean room. Her pale face reflected her resignation to poverty. Her daughter, a young needlewoman, worked diligently at an embroidery frame while her mother made lace. They shared a modest existence, illuminated by a lamp at night.Passersby wondered how the girl maintained her color in such darkness. The mother, hopeful for her daughter’s future, watched for suitors. One day, she noticed a man in a brown coat who passed by regularly. Encouraged by her mother, the girl grew curious about him. When he appeared, he was tall and solemn, stirring feelings in Caroline that intertwined her mother’s hopes with her own emotions.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################For over two months, the mysterious "Black Gentleman" avoided the Rue du Tourniquet, where Madame Crochard and her daughter Caroline lived. While Madame Crochard sought his attention, it was Caroline's vibrant presence that ultimately captivated him, leading to a silent connection through exchanged glances. He began passing by regularly, and their mutual interest grew through small acts of kindness.As winter progressed, their encounters became a source of comfort. Caroline sensed the Gentleman’s troubles, while he noted her struggles with work and finances. Despite their bond, they remained cautious, avoiding conversation and the revelation of feelings, perhaps fearing disappointment.Madame Crochard, frustrated with her daughter's indecisiveness, showed signs of bitterness, especially as winter brought financial strain. One night, the Gentleman overheard their dire situation and, moved by their plight, secretly left money for Caroline, who expressed her gratitude through a gesture he pretended not to notice.In May, Caroline suggested a trip to Montmorency, and the Gentleman, now identified as Roger, unexpectedly joined them. During the outing, he was captivated by Caroline's beauty and innocence, and their conversation flowed easily, revealing shared hopes and dreams. Caroline spoke fondly of her mother’s sacrifices and her desire for a better life, while Roger was drawn to her genuine spirit.As they explored the picturesque landscape, their connection deepened. Caroline's laughter brought joy to Roger, who had previously been burdened by sadness. Their interactions became more intimate, and by the time they reached Saint-Leu, Caroline affectionately called him "Monsieur Roger."Madame Crochard, initially skeptical, eventually joined in their enjoyment, though she remained somewhat oblivious to the budding romance. After a delightful meal, the trio attended a village ball, where Caroline and Roger danced together, their hands intertwined in a moment of shared happiness.As the day ended, Roger expressed a hint of sadness, prompting Caroline to question why happiness should be confined to their outing. They playfully ran down the avenue, and in a tender moment, Roger kissed Caroline's hand, solidifying their connection before returning to reality.The story captures the evolving relationship between Caroline and the Black Gentleman, highlighting themes of love, social struggle, and the longing for a better life amidst their challenges. Their silent bond transforms into a deeper connection, suggesting hope for their future despite uncertainties ahead.The narrative shifts to modern Paris, where Roger and Caroline live in a newly furnished apartment on Rue Taitbout, symbolizing their youthful love. Caroline has enjoyed this charming space for over a month, filled with elegant decor.As September 1816 begins, Caroline prepares for Roger's visit, choosing a beautiful green dress trimmed with chinchilla. She eagerly waits on the balcony, hoping to catch a glimpse of him. After a quarter of an hour, her anticipation turns to frustration, but her spirits lift when she finally sees Roger approaching in a tilbury.Upon his arrival, they embrace passionately, thrilled to be reunited after three long days apart. However, Roger must attend a meeting with the Attorney-General, disappointing Caroline. Despite her initial pout, she reassures him that his presence is the only spectacle she desires. Roger suggests she go to the theater with her mother, promising to join her if he can.Caroline playfully interrupts, reminding him that discussing business detracts from their love. In the drawing-room, Roger notices her refurbished embroidery frame. She expresses her desire to contribute her earnings to the poor, feeling grateful for the life Roger has provided. Caroline asks to take on the name "Caroline de Bellefeuille," and upon receiving his approval, she joyfully embraces the idea.As time slips away, Roger reluctantly prepares to leave. They share a tender goodbye, and Caroline watches him depart, savoring the moment as he drives away, leaving her longing for his return.Five years later, Mademoiselle Caroline de Bellefeuille enjoys a peaceful life. In her blue drawing-room, her four-year-old son, Charles, plays with a rocking horse while Caroline, now twenty-four, reflects on her fulfilling role as a mother. She is dedicated to nurturing her children and fostering an appreciation for goodness and beauty, blissfully unaware of societal judgments, and content in her relationship with Roger.On May 6, 1822, the anniversary of a significant day in her life, Caroline prepares for a special dinner, reminiscing about joyful moments in Bellefeuille. Roger arrives, and they share tender moments with their children, including their newborn daughter, Eugenie. He presents Caroline with a marriage portion for Eugenie, discussing the financial disparities between their children based on gender roles.A few days later, Caroline rushes to visit her ailing mother, Madame Crochard, who is surrounded by friends and a priest. Unbeknownst to Caroline, her mother’s friends are scheming to benefit from her potential inheritance. Upon arrival, Caroline learns that her mother has been confessing to the priest, revealing secrets that could impact Caroline's future.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################For two weeks after the Vicomte de Granville's engagement to Mademoiselle Bontems, he observed her character as she prepared her trousseau. Although Angelique rarely discussed religion, she asserted her belief in the Church's teachings when Granville playfully handled her rosary. His attraction sometimes blurred into a mistaken sense of love.On their marriage contract day, Madame Bontems insisted Granville respect Angelique's religious practices, which he agreed to, charmed by her innocence. After their wedding, they moved to Paris, where Angelique encouraged Granville to choose a large ground-floor apartment near a church. Granville, focused on his role as public prosecutor, left home decoration to Angelique, who filled their space with austere furnishings reflecting her strict upbringing.Granville soon found the decor cold and uninviting, contrasting with the vibrant Parisian life he envisioned. Despite his dissatisfaction, he blamed himself for not guiding Angelique better. Their early married life appeared harmonious, with Granville immersed in work while Angelique adhered strictly to her religious practices.As time passed, Granville became aware of the constraints of Angelique's beliefs, especially when she declined social invitations. Their first significant conflict arose when Granville insisted she attend a ball, leading to a bitter argument about her obligations as his wife. The situation worsened when a priest influenced Angelique to adopt a more rigid interpretation of her faith, leading to misunderstandings about their marriage.Granville's attempts to assert authority were met with resistance, culminating in Angelique seeking guidance from the Pope, whose response deepened their divide. Frustrated by the increasing strictness of their household, Granville began to withdraw socially, creating a stifling atmosphere dominated by Angelique's bigotry. Despite having children, Granville felt isolated, unable to share joy with his family, as Angelique's unwavering commitment to her beliefs left him confronting a life devoid of warmth and connection.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Between 1806 and 1825, the Comte and Comtesse de Granville's household was fraught with emotional turmoil despite a lack of significant events. Madame de Granville, feeling her husband's affection wane, remained unchanged, fervently praying for his love while Granville distanced himself, occupying a separate floor. Each morning, the housekeeper would inquire about Granville's well-being, only to receive polite refusals from his valet, indicating his absence due to court obligations.At thirty-five, Madame de Granville awaited her husband's return with a mix of hope and resentment. Her attempts to engage him during rare moments at home often led to tension, as she sought to assert her moral superiority while he grew more detached. When Granville declined a prestigious judicial position in the provinces, speculation among the Countess's friends about his motivations led to troubling insinuations of infidelity.Unaware of her husband's true life, Madame de Granville clung to the belief that he was incapable of wrongdoing. However, her health deteriorated under the strain of suspicion, worsened by her religious fervor. During Lent in 1822, she fell ill, and despite efforts to be more affectionate, her bitterness often resurfaced.In late May, after a brief recovery, she encountered the Abbe Fontanon, who delivered devastating news: Granville had been living with a mistress for seven years and had fathered two children, squandering their family's fortune. This revelation shattered her illusions, prompting a whirlwind of emotions and a determination to confront her husband.Meanwhile, Granville continued his life outside the marriage, maintaining a relationship with Mademoiselle de Bellefeuille, who was grieving her own circumstances. Roger, a friend, expressed hope for a future together, despite complications in his own marriage.Upon learning of her husband's infidelity, Madame de Granville fainted. When she regained consciousness, she confronted Granville, questioning his love and fidelity. He responded coldly, asserting that her devotion to religious duties had neglected his emotional needs, leading to his estrangement.Granville explained that true love requires sacrifice and understanding, which he felt was absent in their marriage. He criticized her for prioritizing her spiritual obligations over their relationship, suggesting that her rigid adherence to virtue had driven him away. The Countess, in despair, pleaded for forgiveness and promised to change, but Granville remained resolute, stating that his love had faded after years of suffering.Their conversation revealed deep-seated issues in their marriage, with Granville expressing that he could not rekindle feelings long extinguished. Despite her pleas, he emphasized that love cannot be commanded or forced, leaving the Countess to grapple with the reality of their fractured relationship.In December 1833, a weary Comte de Granville walked along Rue Gaillon at midnight and noticed a dim light in an attic window. Young Horace Bianchon recognized him and expressed concern for Granville's safety while walking alone at night. Granville explained he had been watching a shadow in the attic, intrigued by the occupant. He claimed indifference to human emotions, preferring connections with plants and animals, while Bianchon urged him to acknowledge the emotional toll of life.Bianchon revealed that the attic housed Caroline Crochard, a woman devoted to a gambler named Solvet, who exploited her love. Caroline had sacrificed her comfort for Solvet, even selling her hair to support him. Initially indifferent, Granville was visibly affected upon hearing Caroline's name, feeling emotions he thought were buried. However, he refused to help her, instead giving a large sum of money to a scavenger with the condition that it be squandered on vices.Upon returning home, Granville found his son, Eugene, waiting for him. Eugene informed him that a young man named Charles Crochard, recently arrested, claimed to be Granville's son. Granville confirmed this and gave Eugene money to assist, wishing to impart wisdom about marriage and the importance of understanding one’s partner, highlighting the consequences of neglecting social responsibilities.The narrative concludes with Granville preparing to leave for Italy, reflecting on familial complexities and the burdens of his past, encapsulating themes of indifference, sacrifice, and the struggle between emotional detachment and the desire for connection.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################In late November 1809, Paris celebrated Napoleon's marriage to an Austrian archduchess, highlighting the contrast between the opulence of the French aristocracy and the Republic's previous austerity. Women, influenced by the frivolity of Louis XV's reign, sought fleeting romances with soldiers amid frequent calls to arms.Luxury, especially diamonds, defined this era. The Comte de Gondreville hosted a lavish gathering to impress Napoleon, who did not attend due to a dispute with Josephine, foreshadowing their divorce. The soirée attracted beautiful women eager to catch the attention of wealthy generals and officials, with families using these events to introduce their daughters to powerful men.The narrative focuses on a Parisian social gathering featuring a mysterious young woman and interactions among General Montcornet, Baron Martial de la Roche-Hugon, and the Comtesse de Vaudremont. Montcornet admires the young lady's delicate beauty, overshadowed by more flamboyant women. The men speculate about her status, with Montcornet suggesting she is likely married or widowed, while Martial argues she seems too youthful for marriage, revealing the competitive nature of their social interactions.Montcornet humorously critiques Martial's engagement to Madame de Vaudremont, advising caution in his flirtations. Jealousy simmers as both men are drawn to the enigmatic woman, who remains isolated despite her beauty. The arrival of the Comtesse de Vaudremont shifts the focus; she is strategic in her social appearances, ensuring she is always seen at her best. Her entrance creates a stir, intensifying the dynamics among her, Soulanges, and Martial.Martial's growing infatuation with the young lady leads him to neglect the Countess, straining their relationship. Montcornet attempts to learn more about the mysterious woman but faces rejection, revealing her sadness. The narrative culminates in a gambling scene where Soulanges, despite earlier turmoil, becomes engrossed in risking his fortune. Montcornet informs him of the Emperor's praise and a likely promotion, but Soulanges, consumed by dark moods, threatens violence if Martial approaches the woman.In the ballroom, Montcornet notices Madame de Vaudremont's agitation over Martial's interest in the mysterious woman. The Countess reveals her jealousy, while Madame de Lansac, an observant dowager, offers cryptic advice against pursuing Martial. The evening buzzes with excitement, revealing the complexities of love and desire among the characters, while Soulanges broods in a corner.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################As the Duchess spoke, both women admired Colonel Montcornet's handsome face. She cautioned her friend against flirtation, emphasizing the importance of not disrupting families and the pain that often follows fleeting passions. The Duchess recounted her own experiences, urging her companion to avoid entanglements with men like Soulanges, whose life had been disrupted by infatuation.Madame de Lansac introduced the Comtesse de Soulanges, who had reluctantly left her sorrowful surroundings. The Duchess pointed out Soulanges, visibly suffering while others celebrated, which struck the Countess and evoked foreboding about her own future. Despite her emotional turmoil, she thanked the Duchess for bringing her to the gathering.The Duchess advised the Countess that women can repel as well as attract men. Meanwhile, Madame de Lansac hinted to Colonel Montcornet that the Countess was available. The Countess, still feeling compassion for Soulanges, resolved to speak with him, but the Duchess cautioned her to focus on finding a suitable husband instead.Martial, known for his charm, approached the Countess, eager to learn about the unknown woman. The Countess playfully warned him that the woman was Madame de Soulanges, whose husband would not welcome advances. Martial dismissed her concerns, confident he could win the Countess's heart despite her marital status.As the evening progressed, the atmosphere was charged with intrigue. The Baron admired Madame de Soulanges but found her distant, unaware of her aunt's intentions for her to attract his attention. Determined to dance with the Countess, Martial approached her, and after some hesitation, she agreed. Their interaction warmed as the quadrille began, with Martial eager to impress.The ball showcased a complex web of emotions and desires as characters navigated relationships amidst societal expectations. The Baron admired Madame de Soulanges, whose beauty rivaled that of Madame de Vaudremont. As they danced, admiration surrounded her, and she engaged in flirtations, capturing Martial's interest.During a dance, Martial jokingly claimed he had won Montcornet's horse, prompting Montcornet to retort about Martial's financial losses. Martial dismissed these concerns, asserting that Madame de Soulanges was worth far more. Whispers circulated about a budding romance between Martial and the Countess, with mixed reactions from onlookers.Feeling elated, Martial took the Countess's hand, sensing mutual attraction. After the quadrille, he encouraged her to leave her secluded corner and enjoy the festivities. Reluctantly, she agreed but hesitated at each doorway, anxious about being seen. Martial reassured her that her husband was not present, and they wandered through the mansion, eventually reaching a lavish picture gallery.In a secluded boudoir, Martial attempted to seduce the Countess, who initially resisted but eventually sat down gracefully. As they admired the room, Martial expressed his feelings, and the Countess playfully took a diamond ring from his finger. He offered it to her as a token of their connection, and she accepted, intrigued by its beauty.However, the mood shifted when she revealed that the ring belonged to her husband, who had borrowed it from her. This stunned Martial, who insisted it had been given to him by Madame de Vaudremont. The Countess explained the ring's journey, leaving Martial speechless as she left the boudoir, laughing, while he remained confused and disheartened.Upon returning home, the Comtesse de Soulanges felt anxiety after witnessing her husband’s distress at the ball. Regretting her aunt's scheme to attend, she felt the weight of societal corruption. At home, her husband questioned her absence, and she explained her desire to see him without revealing herself, which softened his demeanor. Their interaction was tender, and the Count expressed gratitude for her presence. The Countess showed him the diamond ring, claiming it was one she had found, unaware of its true significance. Meanwhile, General Montcornet did not marry Madame de Vaudremont, who tragically perished in a fire at a ball celebrating Napoleon’s marriage, marking the end of their brief connection. This narrative intertwines themes of love, jealousy, and societal expectations, illustrating the complexities of relationships within elite circles.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################**Summary of "Paz" (La Fausse Maitresse) by Honoré de Balzac****Dedication**Dedicated to Comtesse Clara Maffei.**I**In September 1835, Mademoiselle du Rouvre married Comte Adam Mitgislas Laginski, a Polish exile. The Marquis du Rouvre had squandered much of his fortune, but Clementine inherited wealth from her mother’s family. Although Adam faced prejudice in Paris, he rose in society after purchasing a house on rue de la Pepiniere.By 1836, Adam was fashionable, and Clementine became a prominent figure in Parisian society, living in a luxurious home with modern features. After two years of marriage, Clementine grew curious about Captain Paz, who managed their affairs. Adam explained that Paz was a nobleman from a poor branch of his family and a close friend who fought for Poland.Paz managed Adam's finances, ensuring their lavish lifestyle despite limited income. He was devoted to Adam, often sacrificing his own needs, and their friendship was built on mutual respect. Clementine expressed a desire to know Paz better, intrigued by his character. Adam recounted their bond formed through shared hardships during the Polish revolution, emphasizing that their connection transcended social hierarchies.The narrative explores themes of friendship, loyalty, and social complexity in 19th-century Paris, revealing the intricacies of love amid class distinctions. Paz lived a secluded life, largely unnoticed by fashionable circles. Curiosity arose when Comtesse Laginska rode in the Bois de Boulogne with her husband and a stranger. Clementine insisted that Thaddeus Paz join them for dinner and later the opera, compelling him to dress for the occasion. At dinner, Thaddeus acted like a subordinate, frustrating Adam, who tried to encourage him to be more lively.After dinner, Thaddeus agreed to go to the opera but remained detached, disappointing Clementine. Adam defended Thaddeus, suggesting he was merely trying to appear disagreeable. Later, Clementine noticed lights in Thaddeus's apartment, indicating he had not gone to bed.Thaddeus was tormented by unrequited love for Clementine, which intensified upon her return from a trip. He felt joy and suffering, knowing he was essential to the household's stability, especially given Adam's financial irresponsibility. His love for Clementine was profound yet silent, as he struggled to maintain his friendship with Adam while harboring feelings for his wife.Clementine, busy with social obligations, seemed to forget Thaddeus, who returned to his routine. One day, she spotted him in the Champs-Elysées and invited him to dinner. Thaddeus, anxious, accepted and attempted to engage more, sharing stories that captivated Clementine. The evening ended with a moment of intimacy, but he hesitated to confess his feelings.As time passed, Clementine's social life consumed her, yet she occasionally thought of Thaddeus, who remained a steadfast presence. Their interactions sparked a deeper connection, leading her to invite him to dinner again. During this dinner, Thaddeus became more animated, sharing tales of his experiences, and Clementine began to see him in a new light.Meanwhile, Thaddeus became infatuated with a circus performer named Malaga, fabricating a story about his love for her to distract from his feelings for Clementine. He supported Malaga financially but remained emotionally distant. When rumors circulated about their relationship, Malaga confronted him, leading to a fallout when she rejected his support.Caught between his unexpressed love for Clementine and the consequences of his actions with Malaga, Thaddeus faced emotional turmoil. This culminated in a letter from Malaga, pleading for his return and expressing despair over their separation. Reflecting on the situation, Thaddeus found humor in the absurdity of his circumstances, realizing the depth of his feelings for both women and the challenges of his unfulfilled desires.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Clementine returned home, and Paz saw her looking more beautiful than ever. After dinner, a discussion arose between the count and his wife about Thaddeus, who had left a letter from Malaga with Adam. Adam lamented Thaddeus's downfall due to his infatuation with a circus performer, while Clementine expressed disdain for such women. Adam defended Thaddeus, suggesting he would sacrifice Malaga if necessary.Paz, secretly supporting Malaga, was seen by Adam as a loyal friend. Clementine's contempt for Thaddeus grew as she noticed changes in him. Thaddeus, feeling her disdain, fell into despair but found fleeting joy in his admiration for her, leading to his decline.While nursing Adam through a serious illness, Thaddeus suppressed his feelings for Clementine. Unaware of his sacrifices, she began to appreciate his management of her household but still believed he should abandon Malaga.During carnival season, Thaddeus proposed a night out with Malaga, knowing Clementine would be there. Her disappointment deepened upon seeing them together, and she confronted him, demanding he end his relationship with Malaga if he wished to remain in her good graces. Thaddeus struggled between his love for Clementine and loyalty to Malaga.As Adam recovered, Thaddeus received a letter appointing him captain of an expedition to Khiva and decided to leave Paris. Clementine realized her feelings for him too late, and his departure left her with longing and regret. Thaddeus wrote a heartfelt letter expressing his unrequited love, and three years later, Clementine still hoped to see him again, highlighting the complexities of love and societal expectations.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################The Marquise de Listomere represents the Restoration's blend of religious devotion and worldly elegance. Married for seven years to the unremarkable Marquis de Listomere, she maintains a virtuous image to support his political ambitions. Known as "dull weather," the marquis provides a stable home life that aligns with her values.Despite her reserved demeanor, the Marquise commands respect and skillfully deflects flirtatious advances, engaging in scandal-free conversations with agreeable men. Her beauty is subtle, revealing depth when animated.Recently, she danced with Eugène de Rastignac, a charming young man. Their conversation, which shifted from light topics to women's roles, left a lasting impression. Rastignac, captivated by the Marquise, chose not to pursue her further that evening.The next morning, Rastignac reflected on the Marquise while writing letters, interrupted by his friend Horace discussing an expedition to Morea. Meanwhile, the Marquise received a letter from Rastignac. Initially inclined to discard it, she read on, feeling fatigue and intrigue, which challenged her perceptions of propriety.Madame de Listomere resolved to forbid her porter from admitting Rastignac, ultimately burning the love letter, surprising her housemaid, Caroline.At a party hosted by the Marquis de Beauseant, she anticipated Rastignac's arrival. By 2 AM, realizing he would not come, her thoughts underwent a process Stendhal termed "crystallization."Days later, Rastignac discovered a misplaced letter for Madame de Listomere, reflecting on his mistakes. When he finally visited, the porter informed him she was out, but the marquis invited him in. Rastignac noticed her blush, flattering his vanity.Their conversation revealed underlying tensions, with her skepticism about his intentions. When Rastignac inadvertently suggested another woman was the intended recipient of the letter, she dismissed him coldly.Shortly after, Madame de Listomere withdrew from society, claiming illness, while the marquis attributed her absence to stomach inflammation. In reality, she experienced a nervous attack, using it as an excuse to avoid social interactions, highlighting the complexities of romantic pursuits among the young and inexperienced.######################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################In Paris, social gatherings are divided into two types: the official party, marked by superficial pleasantries, and a more intimate gathering that follows, characterized by genuine conversation. The latter, often hosted by women like Madame d’Espard and Mademoiselle des Touches, provides a refuge for authentic French wit, contrasting with the cold luxury of the former.Mademoiselle des Touches’ salon is known for its grace and freedom of expression, where ideas flow freely. One evening, a group, including the charismatic de Marsay, discussed the evolution of women in France since the July Revolution. The lively conversation featured clever repartee and anecdotes, showcasing the unique blend of wit and intellect in Parisian social life. As the night progressed, the atmosphere shifted from formal to relaxed, with guests sharing personal stories.By around two in the morning, the intimate circle remained at the table, enjoying camaraderie. Mademoiselle des Touches insisted on keeping her guests there, believing moving to the drawing-room would disrupt the gathering's spirit.De Marsay recounted a pivotal moment from his youth that shaped his understanding of love and ambition, describing his first love for a captivating widow. A moment of betrayal shattered his idealistic view of love, leading to a profound realization about love and trust. His reflections on love and revenge resonated with the group, sparking deeper discussion about fidelity and societal expectations, ultimately highlighting the fragility of human emotions.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Cured of my cold and unrequited love, I embarked on a new adventure with Charlotte, a charming woman whose beauty contrasted with my former love. I was careful not to quarrel with her, as her skilled deception offered pleasures that true love could not match. Seeking an artist on Rue Boucher to create a keepsake from my hair, I discovered he used false hair, leaving me skeptical about love, though I still believed in pleasure.Two months later, in Charlotte's boudoir, we shared tender moments. In a passionate moment, I asked if she loved me, and she responded with sweet lies of devotion. However, I interrupted by asking when she would marry the Duke, startling her. I suggested she should marry him, as he was wealthy and virtuous, believing she had been faithful to me due to his scruples. Charlotte feigned innocence, insisting she loved only me, but I pressed her on her relationship with the Duke, revealing my suspicions.As our conversation unfolded, Charlotte's demeanor shifted from playful to desperate. She claimed that if I had spoken sooner, we could have been happy together. I maintained it was too late, and she had gone too far with the Duke. I advised her to reconcile with God, as the Duke awaited proof of her fidelity. In a moment of emotional turmoil, she expressed gratitude for my loyalty, but I sensed her manipulation.Despite lingering feelings of betrayal, I found humor in her belief that I would be left heartbroken. I revealed I had made advances to another woman, sending Charlotte into a panic. She lamented the plight of women, claiming they could never be truly loved. I calmly countered her accusations of infidelity, asserting that love was merely a game for men.Charlotte's anger escalated as she accused me of shattering her illusions about love. I acknowledged her grievances but maintained a submissive attitude, recognizing her need for revenge. My retreat from the situation was calculated, and I felt a sense of control over my emotions.The women present seemed offended by my portrayal of their experiences. Lady Dudley defended Charlotte, arguing that she did not see her marriage as an act of infidelity. De Marsay lamented the decline of social distinctions, noting that duchesses and marquises were disappearing, leaving a diluted version of aristocracy. The conversation turned to the changing nature of women in society, with many lamenting the loss of the great lady archetype.The discussion highlighted how modern women, regardless of social standing, were often reduced to mere shadows of their predecessors. The perfect lady, characterized by elegance and grace, lacked the depth of the great ladies of the past. The group reflected on how societal changes had transformed women into figures more concerned with appearances than substance.Blondet noted that bourgeois women often reference their husbands, while upper-class women tend to conceal theirs, highlighting the isolation women experience in social circles. The "perfect lady" captivates with charm but embodies hesitancy and moral ambiguity, reflecting turbulent times.Blondet critiques modern women's intelligence, which often manifests as doubt. He contrasts the virtuous townswoman with the conflicted lady, who struggles with morality and societal expectations. The modern lady's relationship with religion is superficial; she engages in discussions for social cohesion rather than genuine belief.As the dialogue progresses, characters debate whether women have diminished or retained their greatness. The Princesse de Cadignan argues that while society has changed, the essence of passion and devotion remains constant. The Comte de Vandenesse supports this, asserting that contemporary women possess talents comparable to those of the past.The narrative shifts to a poignant story recounted by General de Montriveau about a disastrous event during the 1812 campaign, illustrating the brutal realities of war. His account starkly contrasts earlier discussions about societal roles, serving as a reminder of the chaos and moral dilemmas faced during conflict. The characters' reflections on love, loss, and human relationships resonate deeply, revealing the complexities of being a woman in their era.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################Doctor Monsieur Bianchon recounts the haunting tale of La Grande Bretèche, a dilapidated house near Vendôme, surrounded by a wild garden that hints at its former beauty. The house's decay suggests a tragic history, and Bianchon imagines it as a refuge for a horticulturally passionate gentleman. The overgrown garden symbolizes lost joy, while the house's ruin raises questions about its abandonment.During his stay in Vendôme, Bianchon becomes captivated by the house and often sneaks into the garden, allowing his imagination to weave stories of its past. One evening, he meets local notary Monsieur Regnault, who reveals that the house belongs to the estate of the late Comtesse de Merret. Regnault explains that her will forbids anyone from entering or repairing the house for fifty years after her death, deepening its mystery.Regnault shares the Comtesse's tragic story: after her husband, the Count de Merret, died, she became reclusive, leaving La Grande Bretèche in disarray, leading to rumors about her mental state. On her deathbed, she summoned Regnault to witness her will, intending to leave her estate to the hospital in Vendôme with strict conditions regarding the house.Bianchon reflects on the Comtesse's life and the fate of La Grande Bretèche, becoming engrossed in local gossip. His landlady, Madame Lepas, reveals more about the Comtesse and her husband, portraying a once-happy couple whose relationship soured due to the Count's temper and the Comtesse's beauty. She also shares a secret about a young Spanish prisoner who stayed at her inn during the war, hinting at connections to the Comtesse's fate.

Augustine Guillaume's cousin's tale is worth mentioning. Élodie-Rose, the widow of Maelien, lived a very miserable life. She was born in the same city, Paris, in the same year as Augustine, and grew up happily alongside her, until she got married. She married very early at 16, to a skinny man exactly twice her age with the same hair color and height,  and moved to Albi with him immediately afterward. The last time Augustine and Élodie-Rose saw each other was at Élodie-Rose’s wedding. Élodie-Rose became single again very shortly after and had to continue her life in a town far from her family. She endured a life full of hardship and pain. The six feet tall lady aged very quickly—her dark black hair turned gray, she developed stage 4 glioblastoma, the worst type of brain cancer, and her beautiful hazel eyes grew sad. She lived in Albi for two decades, most of it alone,  and died there a lonely death.


The narrative explores themes of love, loss, and memory, prompting readers to consider how the past shapes the present. In a parallel story, a woman recounts the mysterious disappearance of Count Férédia, a Spanish Grandee. After he fails to return one night, she finds a letter and valuables he left behind, intended for masses of gratitude for his escape. Her husband searches for him and discovers the Count's clothes near the river, suggesting he escaped.The story darkens as the woman suspects the Count's involvement in Madame de Merret's death, grappling with the moral dilemma of keeping his money. Intrigued by Rosalie, the maid who knows secrets about Madame de Merret's death, the narrator seeks the truth. Rosalie reveals that Madame de Merret had been ill, and her husband confronted her one night, leading to a tragic outcome. Believing in her purity, Monsieur de Merret chose not to open a cupboard where a man was hidden, ultimately confining his wife to her room for twenty days, leaving her in despair. The story concludes with the women reflecting on the moral complexities and emotional turmoil within La Grande Bretèche.######################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################In Baronne de Watteville's drawing-room, a prominent social hub in Besançon, the Archbishop frequently visited due to her devout nature. Monsieur de Watteville, from a notorious lineage, married wealthy heiress Mademoiselle de Rupt in 1815, which deepened Madame de Watteville's piety. A turner by trade, Monsieur de Watteville also collected geological specimens, an eccentric hobby. Their home, filled with fine furnishings and exquisite food, showcased their status and attracted notable clergy.During a ceremonial dinner in September 1834, guests welcomed Monsieur l’Abbe de Grancey, who announced a surprising legal victory, crediting young lawyer Monsieur Savaron for his success against a formidable Parisian opponent. This intrigued the guests, especially Madame de Watteville, who inquired about Savaron’s background. The Vicar-General noted Savaron lived nearby, but his origins were unknown, raising curiosity about him.Historically, France and England have influenced each other culturally, particularly in language and fashion. Recently, England introduced terms like "dandy" and "lion" to French, influencing provincial towns like Besançon, where Amedee-Sylvain de Soulas emerged as a local "lion." Amedee, of Spanish descent, sought to elevate his status in a conservative town.Living modestly, Amedee maintained a lavish lifestyle with the help of his stylish servant, Babylas. Despite his extravagant appearance, he struggled financially, relying on a limited income from his father's estate. His social life revolved around impressing the local aristocracy, particularly Rosalie de Watteville, a sheltered young woman from a wealthy family.Amedee's pursuit of Rosalie was complicated by social standings and familial expectations. He aspired to marry her for her dowry, while her mother scrutinized his intentions. Despite his efforts, he faced challenges in gaining their approval, as Rosalie remained indifferent and her mother upheld rigid expectations. Ultimately, Amedee's pursuit reflected broader themes of aspiration and societal complexities in a changing cultural landscape.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Monsieur de Soulas recounted the story of Albert Savaron, who moved from Paris to Besançon, rented an apartment, and registered as a barrister. Mademoiselle de Watteville recognized his noble lineage, but Amedee de Soulas clarified that he was a commoner who had added a bar-sinister to his name to imply nobility.Initially overlooked by the local legal community, Savaron worked diligently in obscurity. He attended early mass but remained enigmatic, with Monsieur de Soulas learning about him through gossip.Savaron's reputation changed when he successfully defended a mentally challenged man accused of forgery, impressing the court and attracting clients. The Vicar-General, Abbe de Grancey, noted Savaron's striking appearance and eloquence, highlighting his calm demeanor despite a passionate nature. His modest living conditions contrasted with his impressive intellect.Madame de Chavoncourt expressed curiosity about Savaron's motivations for coming to Besançon and his lack of community integration. The Abbe speculated that Savaron would eventually make a significant impact.Rosalie de Watteville, intrigued by Savaron, found him more appealing than her superficial suitors. She planned to meet him at church and felt an intense connection after glimpsing him during mass. Encouraged by her fascination, she urged her father to build a Belvedere in their garden to observe Savaron's private life. Meanwhile, Savaron launched a fortnightly publication, the _Eastern Review_, which deepened Rosalie's infatuation.In July 1823, friends Rodolphe and Leopold traveled to Switzerland, where Rodolphe became captivated by Fanny Lovelace in Gersau. He decided to stay while Leopold continued their journey. Rodolphe, the illegitimate son of a nobleman, was passionate and sensitive, contrasting with Leopold, who was set to become a notary in Paris.After breakfast at the Swan Inn, Rodolphe found lodging with the Stopfer couple and learned that Fanny lived modestly with her elderly father. Eager to meet her, he sought permission to visit her gardens, eventually gaining access despite initial suspicion. One evening, while listening to Fanny sing, Rodolphe overheard her speaking Italian with her mute servant, Gina, suspecting they were Italian refugees. When he approached, Gina attacked him, mistaking him for a threat. Fanny and Gina helped him recover, deepening Rodolphe's attraction to Fanny.As Rodolphe healed, he learned of his mother’s death, plunging him into despair. Leopold returned to France, leaving Rodolphe in Gersau, where he found solace in Fanny's company. Their bond deepened as they shared sorrows and dreams. Fanny revealed she was married to an older man, Emilio, but expressed hope for a future together. Rodolphe's feelings for Fanny intensified, admiring her strength and independence, and vowing to wait for her as their love blossomed against the stunning Swiss landscape.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################For twenty days, Rodolphe stayed with the Bergmanns, captivated by Francesca, who embodied youthful imprudence and the complexities of womanhood. He observed her relationship with her husband, Emilio, marked by gratitude and fatherly affection, which intrigued him.Their interactions were filled with secret joys and playful quarrels. One evening, Francesca expressed her longing for luxury, likening it to her love for art. She declined to accompany Rodolphe to Geneva, fearing gossip that could harm Emilio’s reputation. While professing her love for Rodolphe, she emphasized the need for discretion to protect their bond.The next day, after learning the Bergmanns had left, Rodolphe went to Geneva, settling in Eaux-Vives and inquiring about Italian refugees, particularly a bookseller named Lamporani. His curiosity led him to Villa Diodati, where he encountered Francesca in an elegant carriage, revealing her connection to the aristocratic Colonna family.Rodolphe was struck by the realization of Francesca’s true identity, grappling with feelings of inadequacy for loving a princess disguised as a commoner. He sought an audience with Prince Gandolphini, Francesca’s husband, who welcomed him warmly. Despite his desire to see Francesca, Rodolphe returned to his lodgings, consumed by thoughts of their future.When Rodolphe finally saw Francesca at a gathering, her beauty captivated him. Their eyes met, igniting a profound connection. Francesca’s subtle acknowledgment of their bond amidst the social elite deepened Rodolphe’s feelings, solidifying his love for her.As they spent time together, Francesca encouraged Rodolphe to pursue his ambitions while remaining youthful. Their conversations reflected a shared commitment to love faithfully, despite challenges.Francesca’s background as a well-educated heiress, initially destined for a cloistered life, added depth to her character. Her strategic marriage to Prince Gandolphini contrasted with her revolutionary spirit, aligning with Rodolphe’s aspirations.As spring approached, Rodolphe struggled with longing and anxiety. Tension arose when he compared Francesca to a French noblewoman, inadvertently hurting her. Sensing her distress, he sought to comfort her, but she instructed him to leave until they could meet again in Naples, marking a pivotal moment in their relationship.Upon returning to Paris, Rodolphe discovered a portrait of Princess Gandolphini by Schinner, inspired by Francesca's charm. Driven by ambition and love, Rodolphe invested in a venture that failed, costing him three years. After the Villele ministry fell, he sought solace at the Villa Gandolphini in Naples. Just as he was poised for success, the July 1830 revolution struck, but Rodolphe persevered, supported by love.Meanwhile, Mademoiselle de Watteville was consumed by jealousy over Francesca, believing she was the object of Albert's affections. This ignited a fierce desire to win Albert's love, yet she doubted her worthiness. Rosalie's imagination ran wild with schemes to connect with Albert, leading her to confront Mariette about her relationship with Jerome, deepening her obsession. As Albert's legal career flourished, Rosalie grappled with her unrequited love, questioning when she would find someone who truly loved her, highlighting the complexities of love, jealousy, and sacrifice.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################In spring 1835, nineteen-year-old Rosalie sought to surpass the Duchesse d’Argaiolo, driven by her solitude and hidden desires. Albert de Savarus was gaining political traction in Besançon, securing support for an aqueduct restoration that aligned with his ambitions.Savarus, who had represented local priests, concealed his true motives behind a facade of indifference. In October 1834, he financed a merchant for election positioning. The Abbe de Grancey recognized Savarus's potential but noted his hesitation to align with the Wattevilles, fearing it would compromise his neutrality.Rosalie aimed to introduce Savarus to her social circle, suggesting he act as their lawyer amid her father's legal issues to protect her inheritance and strengthen their bond. She attempted to undermine his political ambitions by alerting the Prefect to his Royalist leanings. Despite this, Savarus was nominated, and his eloquence won public support.As the election approached, the Prefect and allies sought to diminish Savarus's backing, creating tension. Despairing over his unrequited love for Francesca, Savarus felt the weight of his ambitions. The Abbe de Grancey then urged him to connect with the Wattevilles, particularly Madame de Chavoncourt, whose daughter Sidonie could provide crucial support, leading Savarus to consider a marriage alliance for votes.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################The following evening, Madame la Baronne de Watteville hosted a gathering of Besançon’s aristocracy to discuss the election, particularly regarding the Rupts' daughter. The former Master of Appeals was to be introduced, with attendees including Madame de Chavoncourt and her daughters. The Abbe de Grancey mingled among the guests, offering sharp political insights.By nine o’clock, Albert de Savarus had not arrived, raising concern. While Madame de Chavoncourt dismissed his delay, Rosalie, the Baroness's daughter, suspected otherwise. Frustration grew, and the Abbe de Grancey speculated that Albert might be dead. They learned from a servant that he had left in a post-chaise, prompting mixed reactions of shock and relief.As the evening continued, only a few guests remained, including Madame de Chavoncourt and the Abbe de Godenars. The Abbe de Grancey received a letter that visibly affected Rosalie, revealing her feelings for Albert. He deduced she was in love with him and discovered that Albert had withdrawn from the election, leaving Rosalie desperate to keep this news secret to protect her sister Sidonie's prospects.The next morning, Mademoiselle de Watteville learned from Mariette about Albert Savaron's sudden disappearance after a visit from an old gentleman from Paris left him pale and agitated. Following their meeting, Savaron quickly arranged for a journey, leaving behind his keys and instructing his servant to find another job. His departure stirred Besançon, leading to the election of Monsieur de Chavoncourt.As time passed, most forgot Albert, except for a few affected by his absence. Rosalie, aware of the truth about the Duc d’Argaiolo's death, grew troubled. The Baroness de Watteville suggested Rosalie marry Monsieur de Soulas, but she adamantly refused.Tension escalated during a summer at les Rouxey, where Rosalie's refusal to marry became public. After Mariette and Jerome's marriage, the Baron improved les Rouxey, which Rosalie took pride in, further aggravating her mother.Tragedy struck when the Baron drowned while retrieving a shell from the lake, deepening the rift between mother and daughter. Grief-stricken, Rosalie contemplated confessing her role in Albert's fate, having intercepted letters between him and the Duchess.The Vicar-General urged her to seek redemption. She resolved to write to Albert's friend, Monsieur Leopold Hannequin, who revealed that Albert had renounced the world and entered a monastery after the heartbreak caused by the Duchess's marriage. The Vicar-General wrote to Albert, who confirmed his decision to remain in the monastery, forgiving Rosalie.Years later, Rosalie's mother married Monsieur de Soulas, and Rosalie, now of age, was encouraged to marry. Despite her mother's attempts to control her, Rosalie remained resolute in her feelings for Albert. She confronted the Duchess at a ball, revealing the truth about Albert's letters and their tragic separation, leaving the Duchess shaken. Rosalie returned to Besançon, feeling a sense of closure, marked by loss, regret, and a quest for redemption.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################**LETTERS OF TWO BRIDES**  **By Honoré de Balzac**  **Translated by R. S. Scott**  **DEDICATION**  To George Sand, whose friendship has been a source of joy amidst my literary challenges. May this work reflect the esteemed company of great names and noble hearts that accompany my writings.---**LETTERS OF TWO BRIDES****FIRST PART****I. LOUISE DE CHAULIEU TO RENEE DE MAUCOMBE. PARIS, September.**  My dearest Renee, I am free! I hope you have escaped the convent as well. My release was ordinary; my aunt, fearing for my health, allowed me to leave after your departure left me despondent. In Paris, I feel our souls are intertwined after our shared dreams. Without you, I felt trapped in monotony, and my aunt cannot understand my longing for life outside the convent.I remember my aunt's parting words, believing I possess a noble spirit to guide my passions. Arriving in Paris, I found no one waiting for me, and my heart sank at the emptiness of my new home, which belonged to my late grandmother. The memories overwhelmed me, but I resolved to make the space my own.I began to write to you, longing for our conversations and details of our lives. I hope you are well and will share everything about your surroundings.**October 19th.**  I met my mother for the first time since my return. She greeted me warmly, treating me as an equal rather than a daughter. Her beauty is striking, and I was charmed by her demeanor. She expressed her desire to be a friend rather than a strict mother. My father was equally affectionate, and I felt a sense of belonging for the first time.I am preparing for my debut into society, and my father has provided me with a generous allowance. I will have a governess, Miss Griffith, to help me navigate this new world. I am excited yet anxious about the changes ahead.**II. THE SAME TO THE SAME November 25th.**  I am beginning to feel at home in my new life. My father has given me a substantial allowance, and I am acquiring new clothes for my social debut. I am eager to shed my convent attire and embrace the elegance of society.Miss Griffith is proving to be a good companion, and I appreciate her guidance. My father has declined a ministerial position, preferring the quieter life of an ambassador. I sense my family is busy with their own lives, leaving me to discover my own path.I have not yet ventured into the streets of Paris, but I am eager to experience the city. My mother is often out, and I find myself alone with Miss Griffith, allowing time to reflect on my new circumstances.**III. THE SAME TO THE SAME December.**  I am ready to enter society! After much preparation, I feel confident in my appearance and demeanor. I have acquired a beautiful wardrobe and am excited for my debut, hoping to present myself as charming and innocent.Despite my excitement, I feel a twinge of regret for my past life in the convent. The transition is overwhelming, and I am aware of the separations that come with social life. My family remains distant, absorbed in their own affairs, while I navigate this new world.I am curious about the allure of society and how it captivates people, keeping them from truly connecting with one another. I hope to uncover the mysteries of my family and the society I am about to enter.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################On December 15th, I drove through the Champs-Elysees and Bois de Boulogne on a beautiful autumn day. Despite my carefully curated appearance, I received little attention from passersby, except for my approving father and mother, who greeted me with a kiss. Griffith scanned the crowd indifferently while I grew frustrated that one man inspected my carriage without noticing me, realizing that beauty in Paris is more common than I expected.The men I observed were generally unattractive, lacking elegance in their fashion, particularly their hats, which resembled truncated columns. I commented to Miss Griffith about their looks, but she defended them as cultivated.At an evening ball, I stayed close to my mother, who attracted all the attention while I was merely a pretext for compliments. My dress, which I thought stunning, paled in comparison to the married women’s finery. The men at the ball did not impress me; they appeared worn and lacked the proud bearing of their ancestors. One man stood out for his beauty and intellect but seemed self-absorbed, suggesting that intellectuals often prioritize ambition over genuine connections.Determined not to let society's indifference affect me, I focused on enjoying the dance. Upon returning home, I shared my experiences with my mother, who advised me to be discreet about my feelings, highlighting the complexities of feminine duplicity and leaving me uncertain about expressing my joy in dancing.Your letter resonated with me as I reflect on our contrasting lives. You are stepping into a vibrant world, while I seek peace at the Castle of Maucombe, where my room remains unchanged, now with a lovely view of the Gemenos valley.A fortnight after my arrival, my family dined with M. de l'Estorade, a wealthy neighbor whose son was lost during Bonaparte's reign. After evading conscription, he joined the Emperor's bodyguard in 1813 but vanished after the Battle of Leipzig. His father, still hopeful, dreamed of his return. Eventually, the son returned from travels through Russia, Poland, and Germany, aided by the French Minister in Berlin. M. de l'Estorade, a small landowner in Provence, has amassed a fortune of about 250,000 livres, excluding his estate.My parents support a match between me and Louis de l'Estorade, especially since I would be acceptable without a dowry.
