Camille expresses sorrow over her unrequited love, and Claude warns her that her strength could repel men.Calyste, still reeling from the revelations, feels pity and admiration for Camille. He kisses her hands, and she implores Claude not to abandon her. Claude reassures her that Calyste's love for Beatrix will protect her from heartbreak. As Calyste leaves, he feels a sense of loss for Camille, despite his growing feelings for Beatrix.The next day, Calyste learns that Camille's carriage has passed, prompting him to ride to Saint-Nazaire. His family speculates about his intentions, with his aunt suggesting he might be meeting Charlotte, a girl they hope he will marry. However, Calyste is preoccupied with thoughts of Beatrix.Upon reaching Saint-Nazaire, Calyste sees Camille and Beatrix bidding farewell to passengers on a steamer. Beatrix's beauty captivates him, while Camille's presence reminds him of his conflicting emotions. He offers to help the Kergarouets with travel arrangements and is surprised by Charlotte's eagerness to see him.As they travel back, Calyste feels the weight of his feelings for Beatrix and his family's expectations. He is torn between his childhood friendship with Charlotte and his burgeoning love for Beatrix. Calyste resolves to visit Les Touches earlier than planned, hoping to encounter Beatrix in the garden.When Calyste finally meets Beatrix, he is struck by her indifference, which only fuels his desire. He confesses that he no longer loves Camille, but Beatrix's response is cautious, reminding him of their commitments. Just as he begins to express his feelings, Camille interrupts, pulling him away and leaving Beatrix confused.During breakfast at Les Touches, Beatrix maintains a gentle indifference toward Calyste, tormenting him. Camille suggests an excursion, but Beatrix hesitates, fearing the implications of being seen with Calyste. Despite Calyste's enthusiasm, Beatrix's refusal dampens his spirits, leaving him despondent.As Calyste grapples with his emotions, he resolves to confront Beatrix and clarify their relationship, intensifying the tension between Calyste, Camille, and Beatrix and setting the stage for further complications in their intertwined lives.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Upon returning home, Calyste became preoccupied with thoughts of Beatrix and doubts about Camille. He wrote a heartfelt letter to Beatrix, expressing his admiration and lamenting his unreciprocated feelings, which now mirrored his past affection for Camille, whom he viewed as a sister. He hoped Beatrix would recognize his love without expectation.Calyste sought advice from the Chevalier du Halga on discreetly delivering his letter. He hurried to Les Touches and slipped it into Beatrix's hand during a visit, feeling both excited and anxious. Days passed without a response, and when Beatrix finally replied, it was a mix of kindness and rejection. She acknowledged his feelings but stated she could not reciprocate due to her commitment to Camille, urging him to appreciate Camille's devotion and seek a more suitable partner.The baroness, concerned about her son's emotional turmoil, suggested he consider marrying Charlotte de Kergarouet to secure the family estate. However, Calyste remained devoted to Beatrix, dismissing his mother's concerns and writing another passionate letter declaring his unwavering love.Beatrix felt inferior to Camille, recognizing her own lack of talent. As Calyste rushed to Les Touches, Beatrix enjoyed his affection, believing it noble to suppress her feelings. Tensions rose during dinner when Beatrix confronted Camille, leading to a heated exchange filled with veiled insults. Their conversation revealed conflicting feelings about love and rivalry, culminating in a realization that they were now adversaries for Calyste's affection, hinting at the impending conflict that would shape their futures.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################At the end of August, the Guerande marshes have a tropical feel as the _paludiers_ harvest salt. The baron admires the landscape and expresses a desire to see it one last time, prompting a local to question if a Guenic can die. Calyste, Camille, and Gasselin arrive, with Calyste introducing his parents to Mademoiselle des Touches and Madame la Marquise de Rochefide, Beatrix. The baron notes Camille's beauty and worries about Calyste's feelings for Beatrix.The group boards a boat to Croisic, where women in traditional attire unload salt while Beatrix and Calyste explore the rocky peninsula. Camille seeks solitude, creating tension between Beatrix and Calyste. At a rocky outcrop, Beatrix reveals her conflict about love and her commitment to Conti. Calyste professes his love, but Beatrix cannot fully reciprocate. Their conversation is interrupted when Beatrix slips, and Calyste rescues her, leading to her being taken to a farmhouse for care.Calyste stays by Beatrix's side, consumed by his feelings, while Camille observes. As Beatrix recovers, she softens towards Calyste, who remains devoted despite her past. Their relationship deepens, but Beatrix's commitment to Conti creates tension, culminating in a moment of joy interrupted by Conti's unexpected arrival, leaving both Calyste and Beatrix grappling with their emotions.Conti's arrival forces Beatrix to leave Calyste, deeply affecting him. Although he considers confronting Conti, he feels overwhelmed. Camille warns Calyste about Conti's manipulative nature. During dinner, Conti feigns cheerfulness while mocking Beatrix's feelings for Calyste, which only Beatrix and Camille recognize. Afterward, Beatrix confides in Camille about her inability to resist Conti.As the evening progresses, Conti performs a song that Calyste interprets as a cruel jab at Beatrix. The next morning, Conti takes Beatrix away, leaving Calyste heartbroken. He learns of her departure from Camille, who tries to console him but fails to reveal Beatrix's true nature.Calyste's grief leads him to isolate himself, while his family believes he is merely withdrawn and attempts to arrange a marriage for him with Charlotte. Calyste remains devoted to Beatrix, rejecting other prospects. His health declines due to heartache, culminating in a fever. In desperation, Calyste's father prays for his recovery. After the baron's passing, Calyste's despair deepens. Just as hope seems lost, Camille visits and offers to take Calyste to Paris in search of Beatrix, rekindling his belief in a possible reunion. The narrative explores themes of love, loss, and the complexities of human relationships, illustrating how passion evokes both ecstasy and despair.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Felicite's love for her son Calyste centers on securing his future. She sells her house to reclaim the du Guenic estates and plans to settle wealth on Sabine de Grandlieu, the youngest daughter of the Duc de Grandlieu. The duke and duchess have arranged marriages for their daughters, with Sabine being the last available. Felicite hopes to shift Calyste's affections from Beatrix to Sabine.During a trip to Paris, Mademoiselle des Touches discusses decorating a new house for Calyste and Sabine. The family is welcomed at the hotel de Grandlieu, where Calyste is encouraged to enjoy the city's social life. He finds himself attracted to Sabine, who resembles Beatrix, and begins to consider marrying her, despite his lingering feelings for Beatrix.On the day of the marriage contract signing, Calyste unexpectedly refuses the benefits offered by Mademoiselle des Touches, shocking the guests. Sabine enters with a letter from Camille Maupin (Beatrix), expressing her love for Calyste and urging him to accept her gifts for his happiness. She acknowledges her sacrifices and implores him to embrace his new life without remorse.After reading the letter, Calyste agrees to sign the marriage contract. The ceremony occurs the following week, and Calyste and Sabine embark on their honeymoon, surrounded by friends and family. However, tension arises as Sabine realizes that Calyste is not entirely free of his past love for Beatrix.In her letters to her mother, Sabine reveals her struggles in adjusting to married life. She loves Calyste deeply but feels he is still emotionally tied to Beatrix. Despite her efforts to follow her mother's advice on maintaining dignity, she grapples with feelings of inadequacy.As they travel through Brittany, Sabine describes the warmth of Calyste's family and the joy of being accepted by the local community. However, she also acknowledges the challenges of her position as a young wife, feeling the weight of expectations and the complexities of love.Sabine's letters reflect her evolving relationship with Calyste as she navigates the balance between devotion and self-respect. She expresses happiness but also fears about Calyste's unresolved feelings for Beatrix.During a visit to Mademoiselle des Touches at her convent, Sabine receives advice on maintaining her marriage. Mademoiselle emphasizes the importance of keeping Calyste emotionally engaged and warns against rekindling past passions. Sabine is torn between her love for Calyste and the need to protect their relationship from the shadows of his past.The story explores themes of love, duty, and the complexities of marriage, highlighting the emotional struggles faced by both Calyste and Sabine as they begin their new life together, with Beatrix's specter looming over their future.In July 1838, newlywed Baronne du Guenic, Sabine, writes to her mother about her conflicting feelings of jealousy and love for Calyste. She grapples with emotional turmoil stemming from Calyste's past relationship with Beatrix de Rochefide, whose memory haunts her. Despite her mother-in-law's warnings about visiting Les Touches, linked to Calyste's history, Sabine's curiosity compels her to explore it, hoping to understand his feelings.At Les Touches, Sabine feels bitterness and intrigue as she observes Calyste's reactions, fearing that his past love for Beatrix still lingers. Her insecurities grow, leading her to suspect that Calyste's affections for her are overshadowed by memories of Beatrix. This suspicion intensifies when she realizes that reminders of Beatrix rekindle Calyste's emotional responses, causing her to question the strength of their marriage.In August, Sabine retreats to the ruins of an old chateau, seeking solace from her fears. She acknowledges Calyste's charm but feels his love for her is not as deep as her love for him. Her internal struggle is compounded by the realization that she is not the only woman in his heart.By September, Sabine's anxieties about Calyste's past consume her thoughts. She longs for reassurance but feels increasingly isolated in her marriage. Despite her efforts to maintain a facade of happiness, she senses that Calyste's heart is not entirely hers.As they return to Paris, Sabine's hopes for a fulfilling life clash with Calyste's indifference. He allows his mother-in-law and sister-in-law to dictate their social life, further alienating her. Caught between societal expectations and her desire for a deeper connection with Calyste, Sabine feels trapped.In October 1839, Sabine gives birth to a son, believing motherhood will strengthen their bond. However, her happiness is short-lived as Calyste becomes absorbed in Parisian distractions, neglecting his duties as a husband and father. Sabine's attempts to engage him in family life are met with indifference, deepening her isolation.In August 1840, during a theater outing, Calyste unexpectedly encounters Beatrix, reigniting old feelings. Their interaction reveals the fragility of Calyste's loyalty to Sabine, as he struggles to reconcile his past with his present. Beatrix, now changed, uses her charm to manipulate Calyste, drawing him back into her orbit.As Calyste becomes increasingly drawn to Beatrix, Sabine grows desperate for clarity and reassurance. Her fears culminate in a confrontation with Calyste, demanding to know the truth about his feelings for Beatrix. As Sabine's health deteriorates due to emotional strain, Calyste must confront the consequences of his actions, leading to misunderstandings that threaten their marriage. Ultimately, Sabine's anguish over Calyste's betrayal forces both characters to reevaluate their relationship, exploring themes of love, jealousy, and the complexities of human emotions amid societal expectations and past entanglements.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################After giving birth, Sabine feels rejuvenated but soon suspects her husband Calyste of infidelity after receiving a letter from her family. When Calyste finds the letter, he dismisses it, but Sabine's anxiety deepens, especially after receiving a letter from Beatrix, Calyste's rival, which heightens her loneliness.To regain Calyste's affection, Sabine tries to appear happy, but the opera's music overwhelms her, prompting her to flee. Surrounded by luxury, she feels inadequate and becomes obsessed with scrutinizing Calyste's actions. Despite her efforts to beautify their home, Calyste remains indifferent.Sabine's emotional strain affects her health, and she confides in her sister Clotilde, fearing family intervention. At a gathering, she warns her sister Athenais about love's dangers, lamenting that her virtues hinder her happiness.With another pregnancy, her fears of losing Calyste to Beatrix intensify, leading her to contemplate suicide, believing her love brings suffering. Meanwhile, her mother, the duchess, learns of Sabine's plight and seeks help from the Abbe Brossette to restore her marriage, highlighting themes of love, jealousy, and societal expectations on women.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Monsieur de Rochefide, recently abandoned by his wife Beatrix, unexpectedly finds joy in bachelorhood. His wealth, inherited and increased through marriage, allows him a comfortable lifestyle, contrasting with the struggles of women in similar situations. Embracing his newfound freedom, Rochefide lacks originality and depth, relying on imitation and others' opinions to navigate social life. His superficial charm makes him popular, but he remains intellectually shallow.Rochefide's life changes when he meets Madame Schontz, a woman of questionable reputation who has faced hardships. Initially known as La Petite-Aurelie, she lost her parents and was educated at Saint-Denis. Her beauty and wit captivate Rochefide, leading to a relationship that evolves through four phases. In the first phase, Rochefide provides Madame Schontz with modest support, including an apartment and allowance. As their bond deepens, he becomes more generous, enhancing her living situation. Madame Schontz, aware of his insecurities, skillfully manages their finances and presents herself as a devoted partner, boosting Rochefide's social standing.In the third phase, she takes on a motherly role for Rochefide's son, managing his investments wisely, leading to significant financial gains. Rochefide increasingly relies on her for emotional support. By the final phase, Madame Schontz is fully entrenched in Rochefide's life, becoming an influential figure. She cultivates a social circle that elevates her status while maintaining a careful balance of power, illustrating the intricate dynamics of love, power, and social status in 19th-century Paris.Driven by ambition and jealousy of Madame du Bruel, Madame Schontz seeks a respectable marriage while secretly competing with Rochefide. Various men, including artists and poets, are suspected of being her suitors, but none understand her true intentions. Couture, a stock-gambler and one of her few loyal friends, proposes to her, while she also considers Fabien du Ronceret, a provincial eager for fame in Paris. Fabien, son of a chief-justice, abandons his judgeship to pursue social status, embodying the era's ambitions. He adopts extravagant behaviors and ingratiates himself with influential figures like Couture to access Madame Schontz’s social circle.Madame Schontz, aware of Fabien's ambitions, toys with his affections while planning her future. She reveals her wealth and desire for a stable marriage, but Fabien hesitates to commit, prompting her to mock him and refocus on Couture, who quickly proposes upon learning of her fortune.Meanwhile, the Duchesse de Grandlieu seeks help from the manipulative Comte Maxime de Trailles to navigate a family situation. Maxime agrees to assist but demands that the duchess accept his future wife into her social circle, outlining a plan that requires significant sacrifices, including sending her son-in-law away to prevent him from seeing Beatrix, who is involved in a complicated love affair. Maxime's reputation as a cunning social player precedes him, and he is determined to manipulate events to his advantage, revealing the moral decay and vanity of their milieu.The story begins with Maxime de Trailles hosting breakfast at Café Anglais with Finot, Couture, and Lousteau to discuss Madame Schontz. Couture shares insights about her, which Maxime values. Later, he meets La Palferine, a young count, on the boulevard des Italiens. They discuss debts, with La Palferine admitting to some but fewer than Maxime. Impressed by La Palferine's spirit, Maxime proposes a partnership: he will lend him money to win over a marquise in exchange for La Palferine's help in securing a fine marriage for himself.Maxime offers La Palferine twenty thousand francs to win the marquise's affection within ten days. Initially skeptical, La Palferine agrees, intrigued by the challenge. Maxime instructs him to impress the marquise, promising that success will grant La Palferine significant influence. Meanwhile, Maxime visits Madame de Grandlieu, who is distressed over her daughter Sabine's unhappiness due to her husband Calyste's absence. Maxime assures her he will help resolve the situation, hinting at a plan involving Madame Schontz.At a dinner hosted by Madame Schontz, notable guests gather, including La Palferine, who receives the money from Maxime. The lavish dinner showcases the opulence of Parisian society. Maxime encourages La Palferine to pursue the marquise, emphasizing the importance of making a strong impression. As the story unfolds, La Palferine successfully captures Beatrix's attention, leading to encounters that heighten the tension between her and Calyste. Feeling neglected by Calyste, Beatrix becomes increasingly drawn to La Palferine, who represents a new and exciting possibility.Calyste struggles with jealousy and insecurity as he witnesses Beatrix's growing interest in La Palferine. The narrative explores themes of love, ambition, and the complexities of relationships within Parisian high society. As the plot progresses, Beatrix grapples with her feelings for both men, torn between loyalty to Calyste and attraction to La Palferine. The story culminates in a dramatic confrontation, revealing the fragility of love and the consequences of desire, ultimately highlighting the transient nature of passion and societal pressures shaping their lives.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################The French often dislike traveling, unlike the English, who embrace it. This aversion stems from the belief that while other countries may be beautiful, France's unique charm—its wit, conversation, and cultural nuances—is irreplaceable.In 1836, two Parisians found themselves in a palazzo in Genoa, where they could still sense Paris's essence. Among the dinner guests hosted by the French Consul-General were Mademoiselle des Touches, painter Léon de Lora, and critic Claude Vignon. Mademoiselle des Touches had traveled to Florence for business and brought Léon to experience Italy, with their stop in Genoa honoring the Consul-General. Despite the evening's allure, Camille felt scrutinized regarding her talent and femininity.The Consul-General, reminiscent of Lord Byron, had married wealthy heiress Onorina Pedrotti. Their affectionate union was overshadowed by his melancholic demeanor. As the evening progressed, conversation turned to literature and women's morality, with the Consul-General sharing a personal story about his journey from innocence to the complexities of adult life, highlighting the tension between public duty and private longing.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################On a Monday, M. le President de Grandville and M. de Serizy met at Comte Octave’s house to discuss political matters, with me as the secretary. The Count had secured my position as Auditor to the Council of State, and we were tasked with examining important documents. As the meeting extended late into the evening, a servant informed us that two priests had been waiting for two hours.Despite initial hesitations, we decided to dine with them. The dinner was enjoyable, filled with engaging stories from the politicians present. My uncle, a priest, provided witty commentary, while Abbe Gaudron, a simple man of faith, added innocent curiosity. They discussed the societal issue of adultery, highlighting the disparity between civil and religious laws. My uncle criticized the Code for treating adultery lightly, suggesting it should be addressed more seriously, akin to historical practices where guilty parties faced severe consequences.The discussion turned to marriage and the role of women in society. Comte Octave argued that the Church's high regard for women necessitated strict penalties for adultery but noted that societal practices had evolved, leading to a disconnect between law and morality. He lamented the dual standards of morality, where hypocrisy was often punished more harshly than the law.As the conversation progressed, the three men shared personal experiences with marriage, revealing their struggles. Comte Octave's reaction to a comment about divorce indicated deep emotional pain, hinting at his troubled marriage. After the priests left, Octave confided in me about his wife’s departure three years prior, revealing its profound impact on him. He described her letter announcing her flight, which he found dignified despite the betrayal.Octave had lived alone since then, and I sensed a complex drama behind his situation. His suffering was evident, and I felt deep admiration for him. Days passed, and I observed Octave's melancholy, realizing that his pain reflected broader societal issues regarding marriage and happiness. One evening, he finally asked me to stay, signaling a readiness to share more about his struggles.This is the story of Count Octave and his wife, Honorine. Upon returning home from college, Octave found Honorine, his father's ward, a beautiful and innocent girl of sixteen, raised by his mother. She dreamed of happiness, viewing life as a perpetual celebration, untouched by harsh realities. Their relationship was pure and brotherly until Octave proposed marriage when she turned seventeen. After a brief delay imposed by Octave's mother, they married when he was twenty-six and she was nineteen.Initially, their marriage adhered to parental traditions, but as Octave introduced Honorine to society, he became more of a teacher than a husband. This dynamic gradually eroded their love, as Octave's authoritative approach stifled Honorine's youthful spirit. Eighteen months after Octave's father died, Honorine left him, driven by an unknown passion or misfortune, leaving behind a farewell letter that shattered Octave.Despite the pain of her departure, Octave continued to love Honorine deeply, living in her memory and struggling with his feelings. He felt no anger towards her, viewing her as a child who had made a mistake. He recognized that his overwhelming love may have suffocated her, blaming himself for not understanding her needs. Octave's love transformed into desperate longing, and he dedicated himself to finding and protecting Honorine, even as she fell into poverty after being abandoned by her lover.Octave orchestrated a plan to care for Honorine from a distance, ensuring she had the means to survive while remaining unaware of his involvement. He arranged for her to live modestly, believing she was earning her living as an artificial flower maker. For years, he watched over her, maintaining a façade of indifference while secretly longing for her return.As time passed, Octave's obsession with Honorine grew, and he became increasingly desperate to reconnect with her. He attempted to write her a letter, but she rejected it without reading it, deepening his despair. Octave's life became a constant struggle between his duties as a lawyer and his unyielding love for Honorine, which consumed him.In a twist of fate, Octave enlisted the help of a young man, Maurice, to act as a liaison between him and Honorine. Maurice moved into the gardener's cottage next to Honorine's home, posing as a simple florist. This arrangement allowed Octave to gather information about Honorine's life while maintaining his distance.As Maurice spent time with Honorine, he became enchanted by her beauty and grace. Despite his initial role as a spy, he found himself drawn to her, and their interactions blossomed into friendship. Honorine, believing Maurice to be a harmless eccentric, welcomed him into her life, unaware of his true connection to her past.Their conversations revealed Honorine's struggles and her determination to maintain independence despite hardships. She expressed a desire for freedom and a rejection of societal expectations, which resonated with Maurice. As they grew closer, Maurice grappled with his feelings for Honorine, torn between loyalty to Octave and his burgeoning affection for her.The story unfolds against the backdrop of Parisian society, where love, betrayal, and the quest for happiness intertwine. Octave's relentless pursuit of Honorine's heart, coupled with Maurice's growing attachment to her, sets the stage for a complex emotional drama. The narrative explores themes of love, sacrifice, and the consequences of choices made in the name of passion. Ultimately, Octave's unwavering devotion to Honorine and his desire to protect her from the world's cruelties drive the plot forward, revealing the complexities of love and the lengths one will go to for those they cherish.The narrative unfolds on the eve of a significant battle, focusing on Octave and Honorine, who are anxious about their fates. The protagonist meets the Countess, who reveals that her husband, Count Octave, has orchestrated her seclusion under the guise of another's ownership, ensuring her comfort while she believes herself independent.As their conversation deepens, the Countess expresses her desire to escape her marriage, feeling trapped. She confesses her past love for another man, complicating her feelings toward Octave. Despite his kindness, she cannot love him again, believing her past actions have irrevocably changed her identity. The protagonist offers to help her find a way out, but she insists that a convent is her only refuge, fearing Octave's influence could follow her even there.The Countess grapples with her emotions and societal expectations of marriage. She acknowledges the burden of her conscience and the religious implications of her situation, feeling that returning to Octave would mean sacrificing her true self. The protagonist urges her to consider Octave's unwavering devotion and the possibility of reconciliation, but she remains resolute, believing she cannot return without losing her dignity.In a poignant letter to the protagonist, the Countess articulates her turmoil, recognizing societal and religious pressures to return to her husband while rejecting a life devoid of love. She reflects on her past, the child she lost, and the emotional scars that prevent her from embracing a future with Octave. Her words convey deep sorrow as she contemplates the impossibility of rekindling love after betrayal.Unaware of his wife's suffering, the Count is filled with hope upon receiving her letter, believing their reunion is possible. However, the protagonist warns him of the delicate nature of the situation. The Countess ultimately reads Octave's response, which reveals his understanding and compassion, offering her a life of freedom and respect rather than obligation.As the story progresses, the Countess grapples with her identity and societal expectations. She acknowledges her love for Octave but insists it cannot manifest as he desires. The tension between duty and desire culminates in her decision to remain in her current state, despite the Count's willingness to accept her back.The narrative concludes with the Countess's tragic fate as she succumbs to illness, reflecting on her life and sacrifices. Her letters reveal a profound struggle with memory and identity as she confronts the consequences of her choices. The Count, now burdened with guilt, reflects on the complexities of love and the sacrifices made for societal expectations. Ultimately, the story explores themes of love, sacrifice, and the struggle for personal autonomy within societal norms, prompting readers to ponder the true nature of virtue and the cost of happiness.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################The story begins in a lawyer's office where clerk Simonnin mocks an old man, Colonel Chabert, who is believed dead after the battle of Eylau. Despite the clerks' ridicule, Chabert insists on waiting to speak with attorney M. Derville, demonstrating his determination. When he reveals his identity, the clerks are shocked, realizing he is not a beggar but a significant figure. Boucard, the head clerk, suggests Chabert return later, reflecting their indifference to his plight.At Derville's office, Chabert insists on his appointment despite the porter’s indication that Derville is not yet in. When Derville arrives, he is startled by Chabert's ghostly appearance. Chabert recounts his story of commanding a cavalry regiment at Eylau, being gravely injured, and left for dead. He survived, was rescued by a woman, and after months of recovery, sought to reclaim his identity and fortune, only to find his wife, Countess Ferraud, had remarried and inherited his estate.Derville listens intently as Chabert expresses despair over being treated as a madman and longing for vengeance against his wife. Moved by Chabert's plight, Derville offers to help him obtain documents to prove his identity and promises financial assistance. Chabert, grateful, recognizes Derville as a rare ally. Their discussion shifts Chabert's demeanor from despair to determination, and he expresses a willingness to fight for his rights.Three months later, Derville is approached by Crottat, who requests the return of 600 francs given to Chabert. Derville hesitates to continue supporting Chabert financially, recalling that Chabert’s estate was managed by Roguin, a disreputable lawyer. Crottat informs him that Chabert's widow, Rose Chapotel, inherited about 300,000 francs after a contested will. Derville learns that Chabert is living in poor conditions with a dairyman named Vergniaud.Upon visiting Chabert, Derville finds him in squalor, yet Chabert retains his dignity and expresses a desire to reclaim his life and fortune. Derville explains the complexities of legally proving Chabert's identity, warning that his wife may contest his claims. Disheartened by the prospect of a lengthy legal struggle, Chabert agrees to trust Derville with his case.Derville plans to confront Madame Ferraud to gauge her reaction to Chabert's survival. When he meets her, she initially dismisses the idea but becomes defensive when warned of the legal implications. Realizing the gravity of her situation, Madame Ferraud becomes intrigued by leveraging Chabert's affection for a favorable outcome. Derville proposes a compromise, suggesting an amicable settlement that could benefit both parties, confident in navigating the situation to Chabert's advantage.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################A week after Derville's visits, Colonel Chabert and his wife, Madame la Comtesse Ferraud, arrived at Derville's office from different parts of Paris. Chabert, now in a new outfit and wig, exuded soldierly dignity, while the Countess followed, elegantly dressed. The office atmosphere was light, with clerks joking about the unusual situation.Derville prepared a memorandum outlining their agreement on Chabert's identity and the conditions for their marriage. The Countess initially resisted a lawsuit to annul Chabert's death certificate, wanting to avoid legal proceedings. When Chabert entered, tension escalated, revealing the complexities of their relationship. The Countess feigned ignorance about Chabert's identity, but her reaction suggested otherwise.As the discussion progressed, Chabert expressed anger over the Countess's reluctance to acknowledge him and her proposed financial terms. Feeling cornered, she left abruptly, prompting Derville to caution Chabert about her potential traps. Despite his rage, Chabert was deeply affected and left the office in despair.Later, at her country house, the Countess revealed her love for another man, Comte Ferraud. Chabert, though hurt, offered understanding and suggested they maintain dignity to avoid public scandal. During their time together, the Countess attempted to charm Chabert, but her ulterior motives became clear as she plotted to secure her position while keeping him at bay. After several days, Chabert sensed her manipulations, leading to a confrontation with Delbecq, her secretary, who suggested exploiting Chabert's situation for financial gain.Feeling betrayed, Chabert confronted the Countess, who tried to manipulate him further. He ultimately decided to sacrifice his claim to his identity for her happiness, but her true intentions were revealed when she discussed putting him in an asylum if necessary. This shattered Chabert's trust, leading him to reject her and resolve to disappear from her life.Chabert's subsequent life was marked by hardship, falling into poverty and obscurity. Derville, unaware of Chabert's fate, assumed the couple had reached a compromise. He later discovered Chabert imprisoned as a vagabond, stripped of his dignity. Despite his circumstances, Chabert maintained pride and contempt for societal norms.In June 1840, attorney Godeschal visited Bicetre with Derville, where they encountered an elderly pauper drying his handkerchief. Derville revealed he was Colonel Chabert, sent to the workhouse due to reminders of their humble beginnings. Two days later, they returned to find Chabert drawing in the sand, claiming to be "Hyacinthe." An observing pauper noted that despite Chabert's apparent childishness, he was clever and philosophical. Derville lamented the fate of men like Chabert, who served Napoleon only to end up in a workhouse, reflecting on the disillusionment faced by those in the legal profession. Godeschal, having encountered similar cases, expressed a desire to escape the grim realities of Paris for a quieter life in the countryside, critiquing societal indifference towards those who have fallen from grace.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################Bianchon, a prominent Parisian physician, trained under the complex Desplein, who struggled with his atheism and the desire to leave a legacy. Desplein rejected the soul's existence, which he never found in dissections, leading to mixed judgments about his character; he was both generous and miserly, often disdainful of humanity.Bianchon, who faced poverty during his medical studies, distinguished himself through integrity and loyalty. Desplein recognized his potential and mentored him in medicine and Parisian society. Bianchon was intrigued by Desplein's church visit, given his atheism. At dinner, Desplein dismissed the significance of mass, criticizing its historical context.When questioned about his church attendance, Desplein offered vague excuses. Bianchon later confirmed Desplein had founded the service, deepening the mystery of his rituals contradicting his beliefs.Bianchon took seven years to recount a significant incident in Desplein's life. After the 1830 Revolution, amidst chaos, Bianchon joined Desplein at Saint-Sulpice for mass, where Desplein shared his struggles with poverty while financing his education, often surviving on stale bread soaked in milk.Despite hardships, Desplein was determined to succeed, feeling misunderstood by those unaware of true poverty. Facing eviction, his neighbor Bourgeat, a humble water-carrier, helped him financially. Their bond deepened, and when Bourgeat fell ill, Desplein cared for him until his death. Heartbroken, Desplein honored Bourgeat's faith by arranging masses for his soul, prompting Bianchon to reflect on whether Bourgeat's faith might have opened Heaven's gates for Desplein.######################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################In 1828, Dr. Horace Bianchon and Baron de Rastignac walked from Rue du Faubourg Saint-Honoré to the boulevard, discussing Rastignac's admiration for the Marquise d’Espard. Bianchon cautioned him against pursuing her, likening her to a “one-eyed horse” and suggesting she was not as youthful as he believed.Rastignac argued that her wealth and status were appealing, especially since he was financially strained after marrying off his sisters. Bianchon criticized fashionable women for lacking genuine character and warned that the Marquise might be in debt despite her charm.He highlighted high society's superficiality, asserting that true virtue is rare among fashionable women. Rastignac countered that such women could offer significant advantages for an ambitious man.Determined to marry the Marquise, Rastignac prioritized her social connections. Bianchon reluctantly agreed to introduce him to his uncle, Judge Jean-Jules Popinot, known for his strict legal adherence.The next morning, Bianchon visited Popinot, who lived in a dilapidated area of Paris. Despite his skills, the judge was overlooked for promotions, illustrating the struggles of a good man in a flawed system where ambition often overshadows merit. Bianchon contemplated the challenges of persuading Popinot to assist Rastignac, reflecting on ambition and moral dilemmas in the pursuit of success.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Twelve years earlier, in 1816, Popinot was appointed President of the Commission Extraordinary to distribute food during a severe scarcity. Initially hesitant, he became dedicated to alleviating local suffering, evolving from a judge focused on legal outcomes to a compassionate figure akin to Saint Vincent de Paul. His charitable work drained his resources, yet he found fulfillment as a protector and benefactor.By the end of his second year, Popinot had transformed his storeroom into a parlor for the needy, meticulously recording donations and requests for assistance. His aide, Lavienne, helped manage the influx of desperate individuals seeking aid, filling the room from dawn until late morning. Despite the stifling atmosphere, the poor revered Popinot, who tirelessly provided food, shelter, and employment.Living frugally on a modest inherited fortune, Popinot was known for his absent-mindedness regarding personal appearance, often linked to his intellectual pursuits. He remained unrecognized by his peers, having not received the Legion of Honor.The narrative shifts to a case involving the Marquis d’Espard, whose wife sought a Commission in Lunacy for him. Bianchon, a doctor and Popinot's nephew, visits the Rue du Fouarre, where he finds his uncle amidst a crowd of impoverished individuals.Popinot's disheveled attire reflects his preoccupation with charity as he engages with petitioners. Bianchon expresses concern about the Marquis, and Popinot reads the petition detailing the Marquis's alleged mental decline and the influence of a woman named Jeanrenaud. While skeptical, Popinot acknowledges the need for further investigation and reflects on the Marquise's potential ulterior motives. He agrees to visit Madame d’Espard for more information.The following evening, Bianchon finds Popinot absorbed in a complex judgment. They enter the Marquise’s room, where she sits elegantly, flanked by Rastignac and the silent Chevalier d’Espard. Madame d’Espard, notable since her separation from her husband in 1815, maintains a youthful appearance and social connections.As the conversation unfolds, Madame d’Espard recounts her version of events, portraying herself as a victim. Popinot questions her motives, noting her luxurious lifestyle, which seems at odds with her claims of financial distress. Tension escalates as he hints that her request might be driven by self-interest rather than genuine concern for her children. After their meeting, Popinot resolves to investigate further, planning to summon Madame Jeanrenaud, linked to M. d’Espard. The next day, Madame Jeanrenaud arrives, presenting herself as an unassuming figure, far from the seductive temptress implied by the Marquise. She expresses concern for M. d’Espard, intriguing Popinot about the true dynamics at play.As the story unfolds, it becomes clear that the Marquise’s polished exterior masks a web of ambition and manipulation, while Popinot must navigate these treacherous waters with caution. The case promises to reveal not only the truth about M. d’Espard’s mental state but also the lengths individuals will go to protect their interests in Parisian society.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################The text examines the life of M. le Marquis d’Espard, a nobleman living in a dilapidated Paris mansion, illustrating how minor events can lead to significant consequences. A minor illness delayed an important meeting with a judge, reflecting historical instances where small incidents had major repercussions.D’Espard's once-grand home symbolizes the decline of French nobility. Chosen for its garden to benefit his children's health, the mansion, despite its condition, exuded a peaceful atmosphere with modest decor. His private family life was supported by a few loyal servants. D’Espard’s choice to isolate his children led to misunderstandings with neighbors, who viewed him as eccentric. Gossip portrayed him as mad, fueled by his frugal lifestyle and perceived financial ruin, exacerbated by tenants’ jealousy of his noble status, deemed undeserved without wealth.D’Espard and his wife embodied true aristocratic values, instilling dignity and pride in their children, who reflected their noble heritage. At around fifty, the Marquis appeared refined but was seen as cold due to his mannerisms. His children, Comte Clement and Vicomte Camille, were handsome, with Clement nearing sixteen and Camille still in education. The narrative highlights the complexities of social status, isolation, and societal perceptions of declining nobility in a changing France.On a Thursday holiday, Popinot planned to question M. d’Espard. Early that morning, his sons played in the garden, waking their father, who enjoyed watching them. Upon Popinot's arrival, the portress informed him of the Marquis' supposed madness, claiming he encouraged his children to fight. Popinot met the Marquis in a shabby office, where he explained he was investigating a petition for a Commission in Lunacy filed by the Marquise d’Espard. The Marquis expressed concern about how the inquiry might affect his wife and revealed a family secret regarding the Jeanrenaud fortune, which had been wrongfully acquired. He felt compelled to repay the Jeanrenauds, straining his finances.As their conversation continued, the Marquis invited Popinot to his home, revealing he had rented the office for a successful publishing venture. Just then, his sons returned, illustrating their strong bond. Madame Jeanrenaud soon entered, wanting to return the money given to the Marquis, fearing for their honor. Popinot intervened, explaining that the payments were legally justified.Afterward, Popinot reflected on the encounter and the nobility of the Marquis. The next day, he was informed he would be removed from the case due to a potential conflict of interest stemming from his visit with Madame d’Espard. Although Popinot protested, he accepted the decision, recognizing the importance of maintaining the Court's integrity. The narrative explores themes of honor, familial duty, and the complexities of social standing as the Marquis d’Espard grapples with his family's dark history while striving to restore their honor through restitution and dedication to his children.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################Following the 1830 revolution, the art exhibition scene underwent significant changes, leading to viewer dissatisfaction. The once-respected Salon became chaotic, overwhelmed by submissions that diluted quality and made it hard for talented artists to stand out. Complaints about unrecognized talent persisted, while mediocre works gained acceptance due to a lack of a discerning jury, threatening artistic standards.Pierre Grassou, a lesser-known painter from Fougeres, exemplifies the struggles of many artists. In 1832, he lived in a modest Paris studio, content as a genre painter without aspirations for high art. His fortunes changed when Elie Magus, a savvy picture dealer, proposed that Grassou paint portraits of the bourgeois Vervelle family, intrigued by their daughter's substantial dowry.Grassou's background reveals a diligent but unremarkable artist. He studied under various masters, including Servin and Schinner, but never distinguished himself. After moving to Paris, he faced rejection at his first exhibition in 1819, where his painting failed to impress the jury. Instead of despairing, Grassou sought feedback from Schinner, who advised him to abandon painting due to his lack of talent. Undeterred, Grassou revised his work and eventually sold it to Magus for a meager sum, marking the start of a challenging career filled with modest success.For several days, Pierre lingered near Elie's shop, hoping for recognition of his painting, which ultimately vanished. When he inquired, Elie informed him it had sold and offered to buy more of his work. Overjoyed, Pierre felt validated despite skepticism from others. Inspired, he returned to his studio and produced four new paintings, though they were critiqued as imitative. Despite the criticism, Elie bought the paintings for a modest price, allowing Pierre to live frugally while continuing to create art. After years of hard work, he gained recognition at the 1829 Exhibition, where a painting of a condemned man garnered significant attention and was purchased by Madame la Dauphine, marking a turning point in his career.Now financially stable, Pierre furnished a studio and continued to produce art, remaining humble and dedicated to his craft. His friends admired his perseverance, even if they found his work lacking in originality. He maintained a simple lifestyle, investing his earnings with a notary, Cardot, while dreaming of greater artistic achievements.The wealthy Vervelle family approached Pierre for portraits, flattering him, especially as he became drawn to their daughter, Virginie. As he painted their portraits, he grew enchanted by her, despite initial reservations about their social status. The Vervelles, impressed by his reputation and stability, began to consider him a potential match for their daughter.As sittings progressed, the Vervelles grew more familiar with Pierre, who reciprocated their enthusiasm. Their discussions about art solidified their interest in him, and the notary's positive remarks about his character further enhanced their favorable view, making them more inclined to see him as a suitable partner for Virginie. Caught up in the charm of the Vervelle family, Pierre envisioned a future with her, rationalizing that marrying into the family could fulfill his dreams of love and success.On the day Pierre began painting Mademoiselle Virginie, Joseph Bridau burst in, frantic and disheveled, seeking 500 francs from Grassou, claiming creditors were after him. The Vervelles were shocked by his chaotic entrance, but Grassou reluctantly agreed to help. Bridau critiqued Grassou's work, urging him to embrace nature in his painting. After Bridau left, Madame Vervelle worried about the impact of his visit on Virginie's portrait, but Grassou reassured her it had improved.Eager to impress, Vervelle invited Grassou to their country house, showcasing their beautiful grounds and preparing a lavish dinner. In their picture gallery, Grassou was astonished to find many of his own paintings misattributed to masters like Rubens and Rembrandt. Feigning admiration, Grassou realized the extent of the deception. Vervelle boasted about the collection's value, while Grassou noted he had sold those paintings to dealer Elie Magus for a fraction of their worth. Impressed, Vervelle promised to double his daughter's dowry if Grassou could prove his claims.Despite the absurdity, Grassou's reputation grew, and he became known as a fine portrait painter, earning a comfortable living and marrying into the Vervelle family. He lived happily with them, maintaining a successful career while grappling with the insecurity of being mocked by fellow artists. Aspiring to join the Academy, he secretly replaced his lesser works in the Vervelle collection with those of more renowned artists, navigating the complexities of mediocrity and ambition in the art world.######################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################In provincial towns, certain houses evoke deep melancholy, reminiscent of cloisters or ruins. One such house in Saumur, at the end of a steep street leading to the chateau, embodies this sadness. The narrow street, lined with ancient homes, tells the history of France through its carved oaken beams and worn window-sills.Next to a dilapidated artisan's home stands the mansion of Monsieur Grandet, a wealthy country gentleman whose door bears remnants of armorial bearings. The merchants' ground-floors are dark, displaying goods simply. The inhabitants of Saumur, closely tied to the land, discuss the weather's impact on their livelihoods, focusing on harvests and market conditions.On Saturdays, merchants leave for their vineyards, leaving the streets empty. Life unfolds outdoors, with neighbors observing each other closely. The Grandet family, central to this narrative, resides in the old town, once home to local nobility.Monsieur Grandet, known as le Pere Grandet, was a master cooper who became wealthy during the Revolution by purchasing church properties. By 1816, his estate was valued at nearly four million francs, with many believing he had hidden treasures. His reputation stemmed from financial acumen and market manipulation, earning him both admiration and fear.Grandet lived frugally, relying on his land for sustenance and employing only a few trusted individuals. His demeanor was simple yet commanding, and he rarely spoke, preferring short, decisive phrases. His stutter, often mistaken for a lack of education, was a calculated facade. His life revolved around his daughter, Eugenie, whom he intended to marry off advantageously.The competition for Eugenie's hand in marriage involved two families: the Cruchots and the des Grassins. As tensions escalate between these families, the sale of the estate of Froidfond creates further intrigue, as both factions see it as a means to elevate their status. Monsieur Grandet seizes the opportunity to purchase the estate, solidifying his wealth and influence, while the townspeople speculate on its implications for the Grandet family and the ongoing competition for Eugenie’s hand. The fate of Eugenie Grandet reflects the complexities of love, wealth, and familial duty in a changing society.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Monsieur Grandet's house is a cold, tufa stone structure resembling a jail, with a weathered archway and crumbling bas-relief. The entrance features a solid oak door leading to a dark vault and a damp garden enclosed by thick walls. The main room serves multiple purposes—antechamber, salon, office, and dining room—where local townsfolk conduct business with Grandet. Adorned with gray wooden panels and faded tapestries, the hall reflects the family's modest lifestyle.Madame Grandet and their daughter, Eugenie, live under strict conditions, with Grandet tightly controlling their resources. They manage household tasks diligently, often working late into the night. Their servant, La Grande Nanon, has worked for thirty-five years, earning a meager wage but saving for her future. Despite her rough appearance, she is devoted to Grandet, who treats her with disdain yet grudging affection.In November 1819, the family prepares to celebrate Eugenie's birthday. Grandet gifts her a gold coin, while Madame Grandet presents a new dress. Their dinner is interrupted by local notables, including the Cruchots and the des Grassins, who bring gifts for Eugenie. The des Grassins' son, Adolphe, delights her with a workbox, while Grandet remains focused on wealth and marriage prospects for Eugenie.As the evening progresses with card games, tensions among the guests emerge, all vying for Grandet's favor. The atmosphere blends familial warmth with cold calculations of social status and financial gain. The night takes a turn when a loud knock at the door interrupts the festivities, signaling an unexpected visitor.Nanon lit a candle and opened the door for her master, Monsieur Grandet, who was called by his alarmed wife while the players exchanged worried glances. Sensing something ominous, Monsieur des Grassins suggested they leave. When a young man arrived with trunks, Grandet brusquely told his wife to return to the game, leaving the players uneasy.The newcomer, Charles Grandet from Paris, was handsome and stylish, contrasting sharply with the provincial attire of the others. He greeted the company gracefully, but Grandet's stern demeanor overshadowed the welcome. Madame Grandet offered hospitality, but Grandet insisted he needed no refreshment.Visiting Saumur for the first time, Charles aimed to impress with his Parisian sophistication, bringing an extravagant collection of clothing and accessories, unaware of the modest lifestyle that awaited him. The provincial guests were taken aback by his elegance, feeling both admiration and disdain. Eugenie Grandet, Charles's cousin, was particularly captivated, longing for the refinement he represented.As the game of loto concluded, Nanon announced preparations for Charles's room. Eager to impress, Eugenie rushed to help, suggesting improvements for his comfort, even proposing to buy a wax candle, a luxury her father would typically deny.Meanwhile, Madame des Grassins attempted to charm Charles, hinting at social gatherings to alleviate his boredom in Saumur. She subtly criticized Grandet's miserly nature, positioning herself as a potential matchmaker.As the evening progressed, Grandet received a letter revealing a family tragedy: his brother was bankrupt and dying, leaving Charles in a precarious situation. The letter implored Grandet to care for Charles, emphasizing urgency. Grandet's reaction was mixed, concealing his thoughts while contemplating the implications of his brother's demise.After the guests departed, Grandet's demeanor shifted, revealing a man grappling with familial duty and financial responsibility. Charles's arrival disrupts the provincial status quo, igniting curiosity and ambition among the local elite while unveiling the burdens of family legacy and expectations of wealth.Monsieur Grandet, a miser, instructs his nephew Charles on household routines upon his arrival, emphasizing the family's hard work and lack of wealth despite rumors of riches. Charles feels out of place in his uncle's shabby home and is warned about the ferocious dog, questioning why his father sent him there.As Charles settles in, he reflects on his visit's purpose. Meanwhile, Eugenie becomes aware of her feelings for Charles while preparing for the day. She admires her appearance but doubts her beauty, feeling unworthy of him.Eugenie's morning is consumed with thoughts of Charles, and she asks Nanon to prepare a special breakfast to impress him. Grandet, however, is stingy and engages in a comical argument with Nanon over provisions. Despite his frugality, he reluctantly allows Nanon to make a galette for the young ones.Later, Grandet takes Eugenie for a walk, where they meet Cruchot, the notary. Their conversation reveals Grandet's disdain for the idea of marrying Eugenie to Charles, stating he would rather throw her into the Loire than allow such a union. This devastates Eugenie, who has begun to develop feelings for Charles.Grandet soon receives news of his brother's suicide, shocking the family. Eugenie is deeply affected, feeling compassion for Charles, who remains unaware of his father's fate. Grandet insists Eugenie should not inform Charles until he returns, revealing his cold-heartedness.Overwhelmed, Eugenie confides in her mother, who understands but urges her to respect her father's wishes. Despite the tension, Eugenie expresses her affection for Charles and desires to prepare a special breakfast for him, highlighting her growing attachment amid financial hardship and emotional turmoil.After two hours of anxious preparation, Eugenie creates an unusual breakfast for Charles, deviating from their typical meal. She sets the table carefully, worried about her father's reaction if he returned to find such a spread. Madame Grandet reassures her, promising to take the blame if necessary. When Charles arrives, flamboyantly dressed, he greets them cheerfully despite the recent destruction of his family estate.As they eat, Charles expresses a desire for more extravagant food, making Eugenie wish she could provide him with a partridge. Their conversation highlights the contrast between Charles's Parisian lifestyle and Eugenie's simple country life. Charles compliments Eugenie, causing her to blush, and they share a moment of connection. However, the mood shifts when Nanon brings in coffee, prompting Charles to comment on their outdated brewing method.The atmosphere darkens with the familiar knock of Monsieur Grandet. The women panic, quickly hiding evidence of the breakfast. Grandet enters, scrutinizing the scene and chastising them for the lavish meal, unaware of its significance to Charles. As he prepares his own meager breakfast, he notices the sugar and questions its source, leading to a tense silence.Charles, oblivious to the tension, inquires about his father's health, only to learn of his father's suicide. The revelation shatters him, prompting sympathy from Eugenie and her mother, while Grandet remains detached, focusing on financial implications. Moved by Charles's grief, Eugenie feels a growing affection for him, encouraging him to think of his honor as they sew in silence, contemplating wearing mourning for her uncle, indicating her deepening connection to Charles and their shared grief.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################At around four o'clock, a sharp knock startled Madame Grandet. She felt relieved when her husband, Grandet, entered joyfully, announcing he had sold his wine to a Belgian for a good profit. However, when Eugenie suggested helping her grieving cousin Charles, Grandet's mood soured. He scolded her for even considering spending money on him, threatening to send her away if she mentioned it again.Consumed by thoughts of investments, Grandet retreated to his secret den to calculate his wealth. Meanwhile, Charles mourned in his room, refusing to eat. Grandet dismissed the idea of mourning attire, insisting true mourning was internal. The family dinner was tense, with Madame Grandet suggesting they wear mourning, but Grandet scoffed at the expense.Feeling compassion for Charles, Eugenie secretly visited him, offering comfort. She admired his handsome face, now marked by grief, awakening a desire for love she had never known.The next morning, Grandet's demeanor shifted; he appeared more thoughtful and less harsh. He planned to manipulate the situation to his advantage, intending to help Charles while safeguarding his financial interests. He devised a scheme to impress the townspeople without spending his own money.Eugenie busied herself caring for Charles, bringing him breakfast and attending to his needs. Their bond deepened as they shared tender moments amidst their sorrows. Grateful for her kindness, Charles began to see Eugenie in a new light, appreciating her innocence and compassion.As the day progressed, Grandet prepared to host a dinner for the Cruchots, signaling a rare moment of generosity. The household buzzed with activity, and Charles, though still grieving, joined them with quiet dignity. The atmosphere was charged with unspoken emotions, as Eugenie and Charles exchanged glances filled with understanding and hope.The town of Saumur focused on Grandet's dinner for the Cruchots rather than the previous night's controversial wine sale. Grandet, a shrewd miser, disregarded public opinion, viewing himself as superior to the community he often deceived.The des Grassins family learned of Guillaume Grandet's death and decided to visit that evening to express condolences and understand his motives for inviting the Cruchots. The dinner atmosphere was somber, with Grandet grave, Charles silent, and Eugenie and Madame Grandet saying little. After dinner, Charles excused himself to write letters, allowing Grandet to speak privately with the Cruchots.Grandet, known for his cunning, used his stutter and feigned deafness to manipulate the conversation, avoiding responsibility. He discussed bankruptcy with Monsieur C. de Bonfons, who tried to guide him through insolvency complexities. While pretending to be confused, Grandet assessed how to navigate his brother's financial troubles without damaging his own reputation.The Cruchots suggested that Grandet could liquidate his brother's affairs to preserve family honor. However, Grandet expressed reluctance to engage in matters outside his expertise, insisting he was merely a wine-grower focused on his vines. The conversation shifted to the possibility of buying his brother's debts at a reduced rate, which intrigued Grandet but seemed complicated.As the discussion progressed, the notary cautioned against compromising integrity. Just then, the des Grassins arrived, offering condolences for the Grandet family's misfortunes. The notary asserted that Grandet intended to liquidate his brother's debts honorably, surprising the des Grassins, who had expected selfishness.Impressed by Grandet's supposed nobility, the des Grassins offered to help manage the financial situation, but Grandet preferred to work with them rather than the Cruchots. He confided in the banker about his interest in investing in government bonds, indicating a desire to profit while maintaining a façade of familial duty.After the des Grassins left, Grandet instructed his servant Nanon to prepare for a secretive journey to sell his gold in Angers, taking advantage of rising prices due to military preparations. His meticulousness ensured that no one suspected his true intentions as he departed under the cover of night. Grandet's dinner highlighted his manipulation of those around him, reflecting a complex interplay of greed, deception, and the desire to uphold a facade of honor amidst personal gain.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Eugenie stood at the top of the stairs, reflecting on her father’s departure when she heard a cry from her cousin Charles's room. Concerned, she rushed upstairs to find him asleep in an armchair, exhausted from writing letters. She noticed addresses of various businesses, realizing he was preparing to leave France. Her heart sank upon spotting a letter addressed to "My dear Annette," igniting feelings of jealousy and despair.Despite her resolve not to read it, curiosity overwhelmed her. The letter revealed Charles's tragic situation: his father had committed suicide, leaving him destitute. He expressed his determination to seek fortune in the Indies, lamenting that he could not return to Paris, where he would face scorn as the son of a bankrupt. Eugenie felt compassion for him and vowed to help.Continuing to read, she discovered that Charles had no money for his journey or even a proper outfit. He contemplated sailing as a common sailor if necessary. The letter also revealed his deep love for Annette but his decision to part ways for her sake, believing it would be better for both. This filled Eugenie with joy, interpreting it as a sign of his love for her.Eugenie then found another letter addressed to Alphonse, where Charles detailed his financial ruin and requested assistance in settling his affairs. Moved by his situation, Eugenie decided to offer him her savings, nearly six thousand francs. She approached Charles, who was initially hesitant to accept her gift.Eugenie insisted, kneeling before him and imploring him to take the money. Overcome with emotion, Charles eventually agreed, recognizing their deep bond. He then revealed a family heirloom, a dressing case containing portraits of his parents, asking her to keep it safe. Their exchange solidified their connection, transcending mere familial ties.As they shared this intimate moment, both felt a shift in their relationship. Charles began to see hope in their bond, while Eugenie vowed to support him. The next day, they spent time together, their mutual affection growing stronger amidst family struggles.Eugenie's father, Grandet, returned home with news of his financial dealings, having profited from the sale of gold. His jovial demeanor contrasted sharply with Charles's somber reality, highlighting disparities in their lives. As the day unfolded, tension between the characters became palpable. Charles grappled with his newfound responsibilities, while Eugenie remained steadfast in her support. Their relationship blossomed against societal expectations, setting the stage for future challenges.The next day, the Grandet family gathered for breakfast, creating a scene of domestic intimacy. Grief united Madame Grandet, Eugenie, and Charles, while the old man remained indifferent, focused on his avarice. He busied himself with his land and vineyards, allowing the young couple to express their feelings under Madame Grandet's watchful eye.Eugenie experienced the awakening of love, having shared a secret with Charles that deepened their bond. Their relationship blossomed amidst mourning, exchanging tender glances and words. Charles, who had known only tumultuous love, now embraced a pure affection for Eugenie.Three days after des Grassins' departure, Grandet took Charles to the Civil courts to renounce his rights to his father's estate, a painful act for Charles. He also arranged for a passport and sold his belongings, which pleased Grandet. Dressed in mourning clothes, Charles appeared more mature, and the family attended a Requiem for Guillaume Grandet.During breakfast, Charles received letters from Paris, prompting Eugenie to inquire about his affairs. Grandet, irritated by the question, insisted on privacy. Later, in the garden, Charles revealed that he was preparing to leave for the Indies and expressed concern for Eugenie's future, suggesting she might find happiness elsewhere.Eugenie declared her love for him, and they shared a passionate kiss, interrupted by Nanon. As Charles prepared to leave, Grandet showed unexpected generosity, taking an interest in his nephew's departure.On the day of Charles's departure, sadness filled the air. Nanon shed tears for him. The family escorted Charles to the diligence, where Grandet offered parting words of encouragement. As the coach departed, Eugenie wished for divine power to keep Charles close.Meanwhile, Grandet engaged in financial dealings in Paris, acquiring a significant fortune through investments. His cunning allowed him to manage his brother's debts while maintaining his reputation. Over time, creditors grew impatient, but Grandet skillfully delayed payments, ultimately selling his investments for a substantial profit.In the aftermath of Charles's departure, Eugenie faced profound loneliness. She kept Charles's room as it was, hoping for his return. Her beauty transformed, reflecting the depth of her love. She followed his journey on a world map and mourned his absence while maintaining a facade of cheerfulness around others.Eugenie's love became a constant presence in her life, overshadowing her daily activities. Nanon, sympathetic to her plight, expressed frustrations about love, highlighting the sacrifices women make. Despite the pain, Eugenie's devotion to Charles remained unwavering as she navigated her emotions in a world that felt increasingly empty without him.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Two months later, Eugenie and her mother, Madame Grandet, were anxious about a secret involving Charles, Eugenie's cousin. Eugenie often looked at her aunt's portrait, searching for Charles's resemblance. One Sunday, Madame Grandet discovered Eugenie's secret exchange of gold for Charles's affection, leading to panic about facing Grandet on New Year’s Day when he would expect to see Eugenie's gold.As the new year approached, dread filled the women. Madame Grandet suggested avoiding a confrontation with Grandet. On January 1, 1820, she requested a fire in her room to delay their visit. Surprisingly, Grandet agreed, displaying unusual good humor that puzzled them both.Grandet's joy stemmed from a successful financial speculation, having received a substantial sum from Monsieur des Grassins. He reveled in his newfound wealth, unaware of the turmoil within his family. As he prepared for breakfast, he expressed a desire to see Eugenie's gold, heightening the tension. Eugenie suggested they eat first to postpone the confrontation.After breakfast, Grandet pressed Eugenie for her gold, insisting she return it. When she claimed she no longer had it, he erupted in anger, accusing her of deceit. Desperate to protect her mother from Grandet's wrath, Eugenie fell at her mother's feet, pleading for mercy. Madame Grandet, weakened by the confrontation, was taken to bed, leaving Eugenie to face her father alone.Grandet's fury escalated as he demanded to know what Eugenie had done with her gold. She maintained her silence, asserting her independence. Grandet threatened to confine her until she submitted to his authority. Heartbroken, Eugenie retreated to her mother, who tried to comfort her despite her own suffering.The confrontation reached a boiling point, with Grandet cursing Eugenie and accusing her of giving her fortune to Charles. He refused to forgive her, insisting she confess where her gold had gone. Caught between her husband and daughter, Madame Grandet implored him to show compassion, but Grandet remained resolute.As the evening wore on, Grandet dined alone for the first time in years, while Nanon, the servant, tried to comfort Eugenie. The tension in the household was palpable, with Madame Grandet's health deteriorating under the strain. The arrival of guests for New Year’s festivities only highlighted the absence of the women, raising suspicions about the family's troubles.In the quiet of the night, Nanon brought Eugenie a special treat, a hare pâté, determined that she would not suffer on dry bread alone. This small act of kindness amidst the turmoil provided a glimmer of hope for Eugenie, who felt the weight of her choices and her love for Charles. The family dynamics, marked by love, fear, and the struggle for power, set the stage for tragic events.For months, Grandet visited his wife’s room without mentioning Eugenie, who was kept under harsh conditions. As Madame Grandet's health declined, townspeople speculated about the family's situation, eventually learning that Eugenie was being fed only bread and water by her father's orders. Despite the town's condemnation, Eugenie remained unaware of the gossip, focused on her love for Charles and her ailing mother.As her death approached, Madame Grandet urged reconciliation between father and daughter, but Grandet remained indifferent. She confided in the Cruchots about Eugenie's treatment, prompting them to consider legal action against Grandet. However, Eugenie insisted on loyalty to her father, asking them to remain silent to protect family honor.With spring nearing, Madame Grandet's condition worsened, and she sought help from the notary, Cruchot, who warned Grandet that if his wife died, Eugenie would inherit her mother’s estate, potentially forcing him to sell his property. This revelation shocked Grandet, who had never considered the legal implications of his daughter's inheritance. Cruchot advised him to treat Eugenie kindly to avoid conflict over the estate.Realizing the gravity of the situation, Grandet decided to reconcile with Eugenie. However, upon entering their home, he found Eugenie and her mother admiring a dressing-case filled with gold given to Eugenie by Charles. Driven by greed, Grandet attempted to seize the gold, leading to a confrontation where Eugenie threatened self-harm if he did not relent. In a moment of hesitation, Grandet backed down, allowing Eugenie to keep the dressing-case. He then attempted to show affection towards both his wife and daughter, scattering coins on the bed and promising to care for them. Despite his efforts, Madame Grandet passed away peacefully, leaving Eugenie to navigate a world filled with selfishness and sorrow. In her final moments, Madame Grandet imparted a poignant message about happiness, emphasizing that it lies in heaven.The day after her mother’s death, Eugenie felt a renewed attachment to her childhood home, despite its painful memories. Her father, M. Grandet, showed unexpected tenderness, which soon revealed itself as a façade for his self-serving agenda.During a family dinner with notary Maitre Cruchot, M. Grandet informed Eugenie that she was now her mother’s heiress. He pressured her to renounce her rights to the estate, claiming it would spare him taxes and the burden of an inventory. Confused, Eugenie agreed, believing it would ease her father’s worries. M. Grandet promised her a monthly allowance of a hundred francs, which he never fulfilled, instead giving her a few jewels.In the following years, M. Grandet trained Eugenie in managing the household and his finances, instilling his miserly habits in her. Their lives became monotonous, marked by daily routines devoid of joy. Eugenie’s only social interactions were with the Cruchot family and their friends, who played whist with her father. As M. Grandet aged and became frail, Eugenie devoted herself to caring for him after he suffered a stroke.M. Grandet’s death was uneventful, overshadowed by his obsession with hidden wealth. In his final moments, he expressed concern for his treasures rather than for Eugenie. After his passing, she learned from Maitre Cruchot that she inherited a vast fortune, including three hundred thousand francs in income and millions in investments and gold. Yet, her thoughts were consumed by her missing cousin, Charles.Eugenie mourned her father and lost love in solitude, confiding only in her loyal servant, Nanon. Nanon’s marriage brought some joy, but Eugenie remained isolated in her grief. Despite her wealth, she felt no happiness, overshadowed by her mother’s suffering and her unfulfilled love for Charles.As time passed, the Cruchots and other suitors surrounded Eugenie, eager to win her favor and wealth. They flattered her, but she remained indifferent, still longing for Charles, who had not contacted her in seven years, deepening her emotional desolation despite her wealth.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################While events unfolded in Saumur, Charles Grandet amassed wealth in the Indies through the slave trade, usury, and smuggling. Influenced by his cousin Eugenie, he became morally compromised and distanced himself from family. In the Indies, he adopted the alias "Shepherd" to conceal his identity while pursuing wealth. By 1827, he returned to Bordeaux aboard the "Marie Caroline," bringing gold-dust worth nearly two million francs. On the ship, he met Monsieur d’Aubrion, who was financially struggling after marrying into a fashionable family. The d’Aubrions sought a wealthy son-in-law, and Madame d’Aubrion saw potential in Charles.Upon arriving in Paris, Charles grew close to the d’Aubrion family, who aimed to marry him to their unattractive daughter. Madame d’Aubrion promised to help him gain a noble title and a lucrative court position, exciting Charles and overshadowing thoughts of Eugenie.Meanwhile, Eugenie remained in Saumur, reflecting on her past with Charles. She hesitated to open a letter from him, which revealed that he had moved on and was engaged to Mademoiselle d’Aubrion, prioritizing ambition over their childhood promises. Devastated, Eugenie grappled with feelings of loss, recalling her mother’s words about suffering and resigning herself to a life of quiet anguish.Eugenie returned from the garden, avoiding memories of Charles. The parish priest urged her to consider marriage to fulfill societal obligations. Overwhelmed, she expressed a desire to retreat from the world and devote herself to God.
