Madame des Grassins then interrupted with news of Charles's engagement, stirring turmoil within Eugenie. She read a letter detailing Charles's financial troubles and felt betrayed.Later, Eugenie asked the priest if it would be a sin to remain a virgin after marriage, revealing her conflicted feelings. At an evening gathering, guests were curious about her reaction to Charles's return. Despite their scrutiny, she maintained composure, hiding her pain. She made a surprising declaration to Monsieur de Bonfons, asking him to stay, which guests interpreted as a promise of marriage, solidifying her social position.Eugenie stipulated that he must leave her free and not claim marital rights, asking him to pay off her uncle's creditors in exchange for her hand. The president agreed and hurried to settle her debts. Upon his return, he presented Eugenie with the receipts, and she expressed gratitude, acknowledging their complex relationship.Charles learned of Eugenie's actions and received a letter from her severing ties due to his engagement. She wished him happiness but emphasized that their love could not thrive within societal conventions. The president, now aware of the dynamics, felt a mix of triumph and concern.Eugenie and the president's marriage was announced, and he quickly rose in social standing, becoming a judge and later a deputy. However, his ambitions were cut short when he died shortly after his election, leaving Eugenie a widow. Despite her wealth, she lived modestly, dedicating her life to charitable works and maintaining a simple lifestyle reminiscent of her past.Eugenie's life became a testament to her resilience and compassion as she continued to support the less fortunate while grappling with emotional scars. Though pursued by suitors, including the Marquis de Froidfond, she remained steadfast in her independence, embodying a spirit that transcended societal expectations. Her story reflects the complexities of love, duty, and the pursuit of personal fulfillment amidst societal constraints.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################Honoré De Balzac dedicates "Pierrette" to Mademoiselle Anna Hanska, emphasizing the story's melancholy and the need to confront sorrows for someone dear.In October 1827, sixteen-year-old Jacques Brigaut admires Pierrette Lorrain's renovated house in Lower Provins. He sings a Breton wedding song, and when Pierrette appears, he expresses his wish to settle nearby. However, Pierrette urges him to leave when a noise startles her.Reflecting on her life, Pierrette has endured three years of sorrow since her father, Major Lorrain, died. Her mother’s financial struggles led to a disastrous marriage with a physician, resulting in further ruin. Unable to support her, Pierrette's grandparents sent her to live with the Rogrons in Provins, who became her guardians after receiving a letter from them. This unexpected turn shaped her future, making her dependent on her new guardians.The narrative explores themes of social class, familial duty, and the impact of financial struggles on relationships. Pierrette's fragile health and emotional turmoil reflect broader societal issues, particularly for women in precarious situations. The story foreshadows the challenges she will face with the self-interested Rogrons, examining the intersection of personal aspirations and societal constraints.Pere Rogron, an unattractive and selfish innkeeper, lives with his equally unappealing wife on an annual income of about two thousand francs from their land and inn. After selling the inn, they move into a dilapidated house inherited from Pierrette’s grandfather. The Rogrons have two poorly raised children, leading to their physical and emotional decline, as Rogron provides minimal support, expecting them to fend for themselves.Their daughter, Sylvie, becomes a forewoman in Paris, while her brother, Jerome-Denis, follows a similar path. Together, they save enough to buy a retail establishment called the “Family Sister.” Despite their success, both siblings are unattractive and socially inept, earning a reputation for being miserly.When the Lorrains seek to place their orphaned cousin Pierrette in their care, the siblings view her as a threat to their inheritance and ultimately refuse the request. Four years later, the Rogrons return to Provins, where their actions will significantly impact Pierrette’s life, highlighting themes of selfishness and familial duty.A provincial shopkeeper returning from Paris brings new ideas but soon succumbs to provincial life, transitioning from shopkeeper to bourgeois. The Rogrons exemplify this as they remodel their old house into a fashionable one, aiming to emulate the wealthier bourgeois of Provins. Their renovations reflect their aspirations, with the contractor consulting them on every detail.As they attempt to integrate into local society, the Rogrons reconnect with former acquaintances but struggle with their lack of social grace, resulting in social ostracism. Over time, they grow increasingly lonely and invite Pierrette to live with them, hoping to regain social standing. However, their plans are complicated by Pierrette's unfortunate circumstances and the Rogrons' past misdeeds, leading to further alienation and resentment towards the local elite.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################After her grandparents were hospitalized, Pierrette Lorrain felt burdened by charity until she discovered wealthy relatives. Her childhood friend Brigaut, an apprentice cabinet-maker, offered her money for her journey to Paris, which she accepted, highlighting their bond.In October 1824, at eleven years old, Pierrette left Nantes, entrusted to a diligence conductor who took her to her cousins, Mademoiselle Sylvie and Rogron. Upon arrival, she was met with indifference, treated more like a package than a family member. Despite her modest attire, she radiated innocence and charm, but her cousins were more concerned with the costs of her arrival than her well-being.Sylvie and Rogron's initial surprise at Pierrette's presence quickly turned to criticism of her clothing, as they planned an expensive wardrobe for her. Having known love and freedom in Brittany, Pierrette felt a stark contrast in her new home, where her cousins' harshness stifled her spirit.As she adjusted to her new life, Pierrette faced constant scrutiny from her cousins, who prioritized their financial investment in her clothing over her happiness. Her innocent nature clashed with their coldness, leading to feelings of isolation. Despite attempts to dress her like a little lady, Pierrette's playful spirit often led to wear and tear on her clothes, resulting in further criticism. The Rogrons' harshness transformed their relationship from curiosity to tyranny, imposing rigid expectations.The arrival of Vinet and Colonel Gouraud, who sought to manipulate the Rogrons, complicated Pierrette's situation, deepening their resentment and neglect towards her. As her life in Provins progressed, she became increasingly confined and controlled, losing the joy and freedom she once enjoyed.During a family gathering, Pierrette is coldly dismissed by Sylvie, which wounds her deeply. She longs for the affection of her late grandmother, contrasting it with her new guardians' indifference. Sylvie and Vinet trivialize her feelings, asserting that while her grandmother took her money, they will leave her theirs.Pierrette faces harsh treatment from the Rogrons, leading to feelings of inadequacy and despair. Despite having a writing master, she struggles academically and is often punished for her perceived failures. The only warmth comes from Adele, the servant, but this comfort is stripped away when Sylvie insists that Pierrette take on household duties herself.Over time, Pierrette's health declines under the oppressive atmosphere of the Rogron household. The relentless criticism and absence of affection contribute to her deterioration. As she approaches her first communion, she finds solace in her faith, which offers hope amid her suffering. However, the Rogrons remain indifferent to her spiritual growth, viewing her as an obstacle to their ambitions. Ultimately, the narrative presents a bleak picture of Pierrette's life, marked by neglect and cruelty from her relatives.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################To understand the turmoil in the Rogron family following Jacques Brigaut's arrival, we must first consider his journey to Provins. Troubled by Pierrette's distress, Brigaut fled her home, shaken by her transformation from a lively child to a sickly shadow. He climbed to a vantage point to observe her house, consumed with worry and realizing his deep feelings for her.At sixteen, Brigaut was determined to improve himself for Pierrette's sake. After a brief stint in Paris, he returned to Provins and became a journeyman with Monsieur Frappier, the town's best cabinet-maker. His dedication to his trade and desire to protect Pierrette drove him to work hard, earning a modest living while keeping a watchful eye on her.As Brigaut settled into his new life, he learned about the Rogrons' dubious acquisition of Pierrette's grandfather's property and the oppressive nature of her living situation. He often saw Pierrette in the market, where she appeared burdened and unhappy. Despite his longing to approach her, he sensed her distress and chose to keep his distance.Meanwhile, Pierrette faced her cousin Sylvie's jealousy, which intensified after Brigaut's arrival. Sylvie, an old maid fixated on marrying Colonel Gouraud, grew envious of Pierrette, perceiving a potential romantic connection between her and the colonel. Sylvie's insecurities about aging led her to consult a doctor regarding marriage risks, complicating her feelings further.The dynamics within the Rogron household were tense. Sylvie's jealousy manifested in her treatment of Pierrette, whom she accused of having a secret admirer. Pierrette, innocent and unaware of the implications, remained silent, fearing repercussions. Sylvie's relentless scrutiny exacerbated Pierrette's fragile health.As the story unfolds, Sylvie's jealousy becomes increasingly irrational, leading her to plot against Pierrette, convinced that her cousin threatens her chances with the colonel. The tension culminates in a breakfast scene where accusations and distress reach a breaking point, leaving Pierrette feeling isolated and ill.Ultimately, the narrative explores themes of jealousy, innocence, and complex familial relationships, contrasting Brigaut's love for Pierrette with Sylvie's bitterness, highlighting the destructive nature of envy and its impact on those caught in its web.Colonel Gouraud ceased delivering the “Constitutionnel” to the Rogrons for a month, opting for brief visits instead. Sylvie, eager for his attention, dressed attractively and awaited his arrival with her reluctant brother, Rogron. Upon entering, Gouraud complimented Sylvie, but she feigned concern for her cousin's illness to avoid going out.Gouraud felt uneasy about his partnership with Vinet, who seemed to dominate their political efforts, sidelining him. He was also aware of Rogron's growing infatuation with Bathilde de Chargeboeuf, which made him wary of his own standing. To gauge Rogron's feelings, Gouraud mentioned his interest in Bathilde, provoking jealousy and hostility from Rogron.As Gouraud conversed with Sylvie, he suspected her intentions were influenced by Vinet, who had suggested he marry Pierrette instead. Sylvie aimed to control Pierrette and was suspicious of any connection between her cousin and the colonel. Despite Gouraud's attempts at affection, Sylvie remained calculating, wanting to test his loyalty.Sylvie's jealousy intensified as she noticed Pierrette's growing beauty and began plotting against her cousin. She sent Pierrette to dress nicely for dinner, suspecting the colonel's interest in her. Meanwhile, Bathilde and her mother arrived, with Bathilde intent on winning Rogron's affection, encouraged by Vinet to view marriage as a means to gain power.Tensions rose during the evening's card game, with Sylvie's jealousy flaring when she believed Pierrette had helped Gouraud win. After a confrontation, Pierrette fled, injuring her head. Sylvie, indifferent to her injury, focused on her frustrations with the colonel. The next day, Sylvie cruelly attributed Pierrette's injury to divine punishment for disobedience, revealing her harshness and control over her cousin.Pierrette endured a week of torment from Sylvie, suffering both physically and emotionally while remaining silent about Brigaut's arrival for fear of his safety. Despite her pain, thoughts of Brigaut provided solace, akin to an exile longing for home.Brigaut, aware of Pierrette's struggles, devised a plan to communicate by having her lower a letter from her window at midnight. Their mutual longing allowed them to connect despite their separation. One day in the market, Brigaut slipped a note to Pierrette, expressing his love and urging her to escape her oppressive situation and return to Brittany.Moved by Brigaut's words, Pierrette wrote back, revealing her suffering and desire for freedom. After exchanging letters, she felt renewed hope, believing Brigaut would help her escape her misery.However, Sylvie grew suspicious of Pierrette's newfound happiness and began to monitor her closely. Despite her illness, Pierrette clung to the hope of Brigaut's nightly visits. When she fainted during a gathering, the colonel, who had feelings for her, expressed concern, but Sylvie dismissed it.As tensions escalated, Sylvie discovered Pierrette's secret correspondence and confronted her. In a violent struggle, Sylvie attempted to seize Pierrette's letter, leading to a brutal confrontation that left Pierrette injured. Just as Sylvie's cruelty peaked, Pierrette's grandmother arrived, alerted by Brigaut. The old woman intervened, taking Pierrette away from the oppressive environment.Filled with anguish upon seeing her granddaughter's condition, Pierrette's grandmother vowed to seek medical help and take action against the Rogrons. Meanwhile, Brigaut sought a doctor for Pierrette's recovery. As she recuperated, her grandmother provided comfort and support, promising a better life for them both.Monsieur Martener visited Monsieur Tiphaine to address Pierrette's mistreatment by the Rogrons, which had caused her severe illness. The feud between the Vinet and Tiphaine factions in Provins intensified as the Rogrons spread damaging rumors about the Tiphaine family. Tiphaine viewed Pierrette's situation as a chance to politically undermine the Rogrons.Tiphaine consulted notary Auffray and Martener to strategize on legally removing Pierrette from the Rogrons' guardianship. They planned a Family Council to appoint Auffray as her guardian, aiming for her release. News of Pierrette's distress incited public outrage in Provins.As tensions escalated, Vinet, the Rogrons' lawyer, learned of potential criminal charges against them. He manipulated the Rogrons into agreeing to a marriage contract with Mademoiselle de Chargeboeuf to gain public favor and distract from the allegations. Court proceedings commenced, with the Tiphaines and their supporters rallying around Pierrette, while the Rogrons defended their actions.Pierrette was moved to a safer environment under Auffray's care, where doctors confirmed her injuries resulted from the Rogrons' abuse. A court ruling prevented her return to their home, and despite Vinet's attempts to discredit her, the Family Council decided to remove Rogron as her guardian.As the legal battle continued, Pierrette's health deteriorated. She expressed love for her grandmother and friend Brigaut, forgiving her cousins. Tragically, she passed away, leading to a public funeral that the Rogrons avoided due to backlash.Brigaut, devastated, vowed to seek justice. The legal proceedings against the Rogrons collapsed with Pierrette's death, while Vinet exploited the situation for political gain, critiquing societal indifference and moral decay. In the aftermath, Vinet gained political power, and Brigaut became a soldier, haunted by memories of Pierrette.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################De Balzac dedicates "The Vicar of Tours" to sculptor David, emphasizing the enduring nature of David's work compared to the transient quality of his own literary efforts.In autumn 1826, Abbe Birotteau, a short, corpulent priest nearing sixty, is caught in the rain while returning home from a friend's house. Despite his discomfort, he feels uplifted by the prospect of being nominated as canon at the cathedral of Saint-Gatien, a long-held ambition promising a comfortable life.Birotteau lives with Mademoiselle Gamard, an old maid who boards priests. He has long envied the apartment of his deceased friend, Abbe Chapeloud, who created a beautiful living space filled with books. After Chapeloud's death, Birotteau inherits his library and furniture, which brings him joy but also guilt.As Birotteau settles into his new life, he enjoys Gamard's meticulous care but soon notices signs of neglect, like a lack of fire in his room, leading him to suspect she harbors ill will against him. Despite his good nature, Birotteau struggles to understand her hostility, reflecting on their relationship and realizing he has taken her attentions for granted. Gamard, feeling slighted by his social engagements, withdraws her kindness, creating tension between them.Birotteau's longing for the canonry intensifies, blinding him to the conflict with Gamard. His naivety prevents him from recognizing her resentment, stemming from his social aspirations and failure to appreciate her efforts. The narrative explores themes of envy, ambition, and the fragility of happiness, illustrating how misunderstandings can lead to profound personal conflicts.The next morning, Birotteau awoke thinking of his potential canonry, momentarily forgetting the previous night's ominous signs. He rang for his servant, Marianne, but when she did not come, Abbe Troubert entered after knocking. Troubert, a tall, serious man, contrasted sharply with the jovial Birotteau. Their monthly visits were routine, and Troubert expressed concern over Birotteau's health and prospects for the canonry, unaware of Birotteau's naivety regarding Troubert's ambitions.The two men were opposites: Troubert's somber nature clashed with Birotteau's cheerfulness. Troubert, respected for his austere presence, was expected to be promoted to vicar-general, while Birotteau's robust health made him a less appealing candidate for advancement.Abbe Chapeloud quietly opposed Troubert's rise but maintained a facade of respect towards him. After Mademoiselle Gamard declared war on Birotteau, Troubert visited him to borrow a book. Birotteau, pleased to help, felt grateful for Troubert's kindness, especially since it meant he would have a fire to dress by.As they descended for breakfast, Gamard scolded Birotteau for bringing books into the dining room, souring the atmosphere. Birotteau struggled to engage in conversation, ultimately falling silent as his attempts at small talk were met with disdain from Gamard, leaving him feeling isolated.Gamard embodied the archetype of an old maid, marked by bitterness and unfulfilled desires. Despite her harshness, Birotteau remained oblivious to her malice, caught in a cycle of trying to please her. After breakfast, he sought solace in the church, but upon returning home late for dinner, he discovered that Gamard had manipulated the clock to ensure he missed the meal, exemplifying her vindictive nature. Birotteau's life was increasingly overshadowed by Gamard's petty tyranny.Eight days into the story, Birotteau discovers a plot against him by Mademoiselle Gamard, who has harbored resentment for months. Initially oblivious to her malicious intentions, Birotteau becomes tormented as unsettling incidents reveal he is under her scrutiny. His once peaceful life spirals into despair as he perceives Gamard's vindictiveness as a personal attack.Feeling isolated and ashamed to confide in friends, Birotteau seeks help from Abbe Troubert, who dismisses his concerns as trivial, attributing Gamard's behavior to health issues, which deepens Birotteau's helplessness.To escape, Birotteau plans a short stay at Madame de Listomere's country house, unaware this plays into Gamard's hands. At the Alouette, he is confronted by Monsieur Caron, Gamard's lawyer, who warns that his absence could lead to legal complications regarding his living arrangements. Confused and distressed, Birotteau returns to his friends, who urge him to stand firm against Gamard.Madame de Listomere and others analyze the situation, suspecting Troubert covets Birotteau's apartment. They encourage him to resist Gamard's influence, but Birotteau's naivety leads him to sign a document relinquishing his rights to his belongings in Gamard's house, believing it to be a mere formality. This act seals his fate, as he later learns Gamard intends to keep his possessions as compensation for the difference in board prices.Upon returning to confront Gamard, Birotteau is met with her triumphant demeanor and realizes the extent of his loss. Troubert has taken over his former apartment, and Gamard claims his furniture as part of their agreement. Devastated, Birotteau seeks solace at Madame de Listomere's, where friends promise to help him reclaim his dignity and possessions.Mademoiselle Salomon, a devoted friend, takes Birotteau back to the Alouette, where he struggles to comprehend the betrayal. His friends, including Monsieur de Bourbonne, strategize a legal response against Gamard, believing they can expose her deceit. However, the local lawyer advises against pursuing the case, suggesting Birotteau's actions may be seen as ingratitude.Despite this, Birotteau's friends remain committed to supporting him, recognizing the social implications of the conflict against Gamard and Troubert. As the legal battle unfolds, they gather evidence of the value of Birotteau's possessions, which far exceed what he owed Gamard, illustrating the complexities of human relationships and motivations.A few days later, Baron de Listomere received concerning news about his potential retirement from the Navy. Seeking clarification, he approached the minister, who dismissed his fears. However, further inquiries revealed a document confirming his retirement was pending signature. The baron sought help from his uncle, a deputy, who warned that his political views had made him unpopular, particularly with the influential Abbe Troubert. He advised the baron to reconcile with Troubert to secure his career.Three days later, the baron returned home, anxious about the family's support for Birotteau, whom he considered a liability. He consulted Monsieur de Bourbonne, who suggested distancing themselves from Birotteau and appeasing Troubert. The baroness then asked Birotteau to withdraw his lawsuit against Mademoiselle Gamard and leave her house for family interests. Distressed, Birotteau reluctantly agreed but insisted on retrieving a portrait of Abbe Chapeloud.Madame de Listomere promised to negotiate with Mademoiselle Gamard for the portrait. Shortly after, Gamard fell ill and died, leaving her fortune to Troubert, which led to gossip blaming Birotteau for her death. Madame de Listomere visited Troubert to negotiate peace, presenting Birotteau's relinquishment of claims. Troubert, feigning indifference, refused to part with the portrait, claiming it was a cherished memento.Gamard's death further isolated Birotteau, who was appointed curate of Saint-Symphorien, effectively exiling him. Meanwhile, Troubert ascended to Bishop of Troyes, consolidating his power. After Madame de Listomere's death, Troubert contested Birotteau's annuity, threatening the baron's career. Ultimately, Birotteau's life deteriorated amid illness and despair, while Troubert's influence grew unchecked, highlighting the consequences of ecclesiastical politics in provincial France.######################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################**The Two Brothers**  *By Honoré De Balzac*  *Translated by Katharine Prescott Wormeley*  **Dedication**  To Monsieur Charles Nodier, a member of the French Academy. This book explores the hidden injustices of domestic life, where divine chance often replaces human justice, reflecting on the decline of paternal authority and the consequences of feminine weakness, urging a return to the moral foundations of marriage and family.---**Chapter I**  In 1792, the townspeople of Issoudun viewed Dr. Rouget as malicious, believing he made his beautiful wife, Madame Rouget, unhappy. Their son, Jean-Jacques, was born first, followed by their daughter Agathe a decade later, whose unexpected arrival strained family dynamics. The Rougets were connected to the wealthy Descoings family, who profited from the wool trade. Descoings’ brother sought fortune in Paris as a grocer but ultimately failed.Descoings was imprisoned during the Revolution, leading to his execution, which shocked the town. After his death, Madame Rouget’s health deteriorated due to her husband’s neglect and their son’s indifference. Agathe was sent to Paris to live with her uncle, but her arrival coincided with Descoings’ imprisonment, leaving him unable to care for her.Fearing she would never see Agathe again, Madame Rouget fell ill. Agathe’s beauty attracted Bridau, a secretary in the ministry, who married her with Dr. Rouget’s approval. After inheriting the Descoings’ wealth, Dr. Rouget led a debauched life following his wife’s death in 1799, leaving Jean-Jacques unprepared for adulthood.Agathe, resembling her mother, was a devoted wife and mother, but her ignorance of life’s complexities led to misfortunes. Bridau, loyal to Napoleon, worked tirelessly in the ministry, earning a substantial salary. Their life in Paris was marked by domestic bliss until Bridau’s overwork led to his death in 1808, just before a significant promotion.After Bridau’s death, Agathe received a pension and scholarships for her sons but remained estranged from her family in Issoudun. Despite warnings from her godmother, Madame Hochon, Agathe did not contest her father’s will, which deprived her of her rightful inheritance. Instead, she and Madame Descoings pooled their resources, living modestly on a combined income.Madame Descoings, now sixty-five, maintained her youthful appearance and sought to cheer Agathe, often taking her to the theater. However, she harbored a secret about her grandson, Bixiou, whom she raised in secrecy. Agathe, devoted to her children, declined to remarry, but her lack of authority as a mother led to significant challenges.Madame Descoings’ gambling addiction worsened their financial situation, as she used household funds to support her lottery habit. As debts mounted, Agathe, unaware of the extent of the problem, continued to trust her aunt. Eventually, Madame Descoings confessed her financial troubles to Agathe, who, without reproach, took drastic measures to pay off the debts, selling possessions and cutting expenses.This chapter sets the stage for the unfolding drama of familial relationships, financial struggles, and the consequences of choices made within societal expectations and personal ambitions, highlighting the complexities of love, loyalty, and the often-hidden burdens within families.The rue Mazarin, particularly between rue Guenegard and rue de Seine, is one of Paris's dreariest areas, dominated by Cardinal Mazarin's college and library. Here, Madame Bridau, a widow, lives on the third floor of a dimly lit building. Opposite her, the Institute trains aspiring artists, known as "rapins," who undergo rigorous examinations for prestigious prizes.Madame Bridau's modest apartment, furnished with only essentials, features small gardens despite police regulations. She chose this location for its affordability (400 francs a year), proximity to her sons' school, and familiarity. The interior reflects her frugal lifestyle, with a sparsely decorated dining room and salon, save for a portrait of her late husband and a vibrant painting of Napoleon.Since her husband's death, Madame Bridau has become increasingly disheveled and consumed by grief, neglecting her appearance and home. Her devotion to her late husband is evident in her preservation of his belongings. With financial struggles looming, she worries about her children, Philippe and Joseph, despite receiving some support from the Emperor.Nearby, her aunt Madame Descoings also faces financial difficulties but provides assistance to Madame Bridau. The two widows share a close bond, often playing cards with friends from the ministry. Madame Descoings dotes on Philippe and Joseph, indulging them with treats and small gifts, while Madame Bridau restricts her spending to save for her children's future.Philippe, the elder son, resembles his mother and exhibits a bold demeanor, preferring physical activities over academics. In contrast, Joseph is more introverted and artistic, struggling with cleanliness and often scolded by his mother. Despite their differences, both boys are loved by their mother, though she tends to favor Philippe.Joseph's interest in art is ignited when he sees a student drawing a caricature at the Institute. He becomes enamored with the artistic environment and begins visiting sculptor Chaudet's studio, who recognizes his potential. However, Madame Bridau is distressed by her son's aspirations, fearing he will face hardship as an artist.Despite her objections, Joseph pursues art, encouraged by Madame Descoings. Meanwhile, Philippe, inspired by military grandeur, secretly petitions the Emperor to join the army. He is accepted into the Imperial Lyceum and quickly rises through the ranks, earning accolades for his bravery.As the political landscape shifts following Napoleon's return from exile, both brothers face challenges. Joseph's scholarship is revoked, and he is determined to make a living as an artist, while Philippe, now a captain, returns home to find his family in financial ruin. Their contrasting paths highlight their differing personalities and the complexities of their mother's affections.Madame Bridau's preference for Philippe grows as he embodies the heroic ideals she admires, while Joseph's artistic pursuits cause her anxiety. Despite this, she remains a loving mother, struggling to understand her younger son’s aspirations. The family dynamics reflect broader themes of ambition, sacrifice, and the pursuit of identity amidst societal expectations.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################In 1816, Joseph Bridau transformed his attic into an art studio with support from his mother, Agathe, and financial backing from Madame Descoings. Both widows viewed painting as a respectable profession. Joseph decorated the studio and continued his studies under Gros and Schinner, while political unrest in France led to conspiracies and riots. His brother Philippe became involved in these conspiracies, was arrested, and later released, but his military career suffered when he was placed back on active duty.Agathe worried for Philippe's safety as he frequented disreputable cafes, while the Grecian sages, regular visitors to her home, advised sending him abroad. Despite their concerns, Philippe was determined to serve France and eventually joined General Lallemand in the United States, leading to the ill-fated Champ d’Asile venture. Agathe invested ten thousand francs to support him, but upon his return in 1819, Philippe was a changed man, hardened by his experiences and struggling with alcoholism.Eager to help, Agathe bought him new clothes and indulged his drinking habits, despite her suspicions about his character. Philippe's tales of woe masked his selfishness and brutality, which had developed during his time away. Agathe remained blind to his flaws, believing he was a victim of circumstance.Back in Paris, Philippe's behavior alarmed Agathe and Madame Descoings. They faced financial struggles, and Agathe suggested selling their furniture to support him. Philippe assured them he would find work, but he continued gambling and drinking, worsening their financial strain.Philippe's social life revolved around old army comrades and gambling, becoming increasingly reckless. He formed a bond with Giroudeau, a former dragoon, who introduced him to Paris nightlife. Philippe's ambitions shifted towards pursuing a relationship with Mariette, a young dancer, while still dreaming of political influence.Despite his failures, Philippe sought to leverage his past for personal gain, planning to demand accountability for the funds raised for the Champ d’Asile. His mother and Madame Descoings, desperate for stability, agreed to secure him a cashier position at a new newspaper, believing it would alleviate their financial woes. As Philippe's situation improved, he promised to contribute to the household, renewing Agathe's faith in him. However, Joseph, feeling overshadowed by Philippe's return, resolved to support himself independently.Three months later, Colonel Philippe lived extravagantly without supporting his mother and Madame Descoings, who hesitated to remind him of his obligations. His mother believed he was content. Meanwhile, Mariette gained fame through Philippe and his friends.Bixiou informed Agathe and Madame Descoings of Philippe's infatuation with Mariette, shocking them as they viewed stage women as morally corrupt. Joseph dismissed the idea that Philippe would waste money on her. Bixiou warned that Mariette might attract wealthier suitors.Driven by greed, Philippe proposed marriage to Mariette, but she declined, seeking more lucrative prospects at the Grand Opera. As Philippe distanced himself from his family, he indulged in a hedonistic lifestyle. However, when Mariette found success, Philippe's finances worsened, leading him to embezzle funds from his job.After Mariette left for London, Philippe returned home, grappling with despair over his inability to maintain his lifestyle. He confided in Joseph about his suicidal thoughts stemming from his embezzlement. Alarmed, Joseph urged him to reconsider, suggesting that repayment could avert dishonor.Philippe fled, leaving his family distressed. Agathe and Madame Descoings were devastated upon learning of his actions, fearing for his life. Despite their anguish, Philippe concealed his theft by claiming he had kept the money for safekeeping.He then visited a gambling house, initially winning but ultimately losing everything. Returning home, he feigned distress, and his family welcomed him back. Agathe, still shaken, begged him to promise not to harm himself, and Philippe exploited their concern.In the following days, Agathe visibly aged from worry, realizing their financial situation was dire. Madame Descoings suggested consulting a lawyer to manage the fallout from Philippe's actions. The lawyer devised a plan to protect Philippe’s reputation, framing his theft as a result of misplaced trust.Philippe resumed a life of leisure, gambling, and drinking, while Joseph focused on his art, gaining recognition. Agathe remained preoccupied with Philippe, oscillating between hope and fear regarding his future. As Philippe continued his reckless lifestyle, Joseph suspected Philippe was stealing from him, deepening their struggles and closing the chapter with foreboding about Philippe's potential downfall.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Despite Philippe's cautious gambling, he sometimes found himself "cleaned out." Desperate for his nightly stake of ten francs, he resorted to stealing from his family, including his mother Agathe and Madame Descoings. Agathe, heartbroken, recognized that his thefts indicated a deeper moral failing. After a troubling dream where Philippe robbed her, she urged him to ask for money instead of stealing. However, within two weeks, Agathe was left with nothing and considered finding work to support herself.As Agathe's health deteriorated from worry, Philippe continued his reckless lifestyle, neglecting his appearance and becoming increasingly debauched. Madame Descoings urged him to buy new clothes, but Philippe dismissed her concerns, claiming he had no money. He had even mortgaged his military pension to pay off gambling debts.On Christmas Eve, Philippe planned to steal from Madame Descoings, who had saved money for lottery tickets. He broke into her apartment while she was out, found twenty napoleons hidden in her mattress, and left without raising suspicion. With this money, he gambled and experienced a lucky streak, winning substantial amounts at the gaming house. However, his greed led him to ignore his own gambling rules, and he ultimately lost everything.Meanwhile, Agathe and Madame Descoings awaited Philippe's return for Christmas dinner. When he finally arrived, he was drunk, and tensions escalated as Agathe confronted him about his behavior. Philippe, in a fit of rage, dismissed their concerns, even as Madame Descoings suffered from the shock of losing her savings.The next day, Madame Descoings learned that her lottery ticket had won three million francs, but she was devastated to discover that Philippe had stolen her money before she could pay for her stake. This revelation caused her to suffer a stroke, leaving her incapacitated. Agathe and Joseph were left to deal with the fallout of Philippe's actions, which had brought ruin upon their family.As Madame Descoings lay ill, Philippe returned home, oblivious to the chaos he had caused. Agathe confronted him about his theft and the pain inflicted on their family. Philippe, however, remained indifferent, believing he was justified. The confrontation escalated into violence, leading to Philippe being restrained due to his delirium.In the aftermath, Madame Descoings passed away, leaving Agathe with a meager annuity. Philippe, now estranged from his family, continued his reckless lifestyle, showing no remorse. Agathe, heartbroken and defeated, prayed for her son, realizing that her motherhood had become a source of deep sorrow.By February 1822, Madame Bridau had moved into a modest attic room above her former apartment, where her son Joseph aimed to make her comfortable after Philippe's departure. Joseph decorated the room and took Agathe to dine daily at a table-d’hote, costing nearly a hundred francs a month. Concerned about costs, Agathe secured a job as an assistant in a lottery office, earning seven hundred francs a year, which required long hours.Despite her new routine, Agathe found solace in its stability. She prepared breakfast for Joseph and kept his studio tidy, though she struggled with the artistic discussions around her. Their bond deepened through small acts of care, even as they remained distant regarding art. One day, Agathe expressed concern for Philippe, prompting Joseph to visit him. Philippe, now a cashier for a newspaper, invited Joseph to dinner, but Joseph declined, prioritizing his mother.As Philippe posed for a portrait, Agathe secretly observed, filled with hope for their family. However, Philippe's behavior raised Joseph's suspicions, particularly regarding his finances. After a lavish dinner, Agathe's joy turned to worry when Philippe's reckless spending became evident. During another sitting, Philippe boasted of his ambitions but was evasive about his finances.One evening, Philippe stole a valuable painting from Joseph's studio, believing it to be a copy. When Joseph discovered the theft, he was devastated, and Agathe was heartbroken, realizing the extent of Philippe's betrayal. Her despair deepened upon learning of Philippe's deteriorating condition and need for medical care. Despite Giroudeau's pleas for financial assistance, Agathe refused to help Philippe, feeling betrayed and exhausted.Months later, Agathe encountered Philippe on the street, now a shadow of his former self, recently released from a hospital. Overcome with maternal instinct, she gave him money, despite knowing it would likely be squandered. Her health declined as she grappled with the pain of her son's choices.When news broke of Philippe's involvement in a conspiracy, Agathe fainted, fearing for his life. Joseph sought legal advice from Desroches, who advised them to secure their family's inheritance from Philippe's influence over their wealthy uncle, Jean-Jacques Rouget. Agathe wrote to her godmother, Madame Hochon, seeking help, but learned that Rouget was unlikely to assist. Desroches urged Agathe to travel to Issoudun to protect her family's interests, prompting her to reflect on the changes in Issoudun and her estrangement from her brother.Issoudun, one of France's oldest cities, has a rich history and significant commerce, highlighted by archaeological finds like a fifth-century basilica on a Roman temple site. Its name, derived from "Dun," suggests Celtic origins and a long-standing settlement. Historically a wool trade center, Issoudun had a population of around 16,000 in the fourteenth century, but this has declined to about 12,000 today, including vine-growers from surrounding areas.This decline is largely due to residents' resistance to change, particularly in viticulture, where outdated practices hinder quality wine production. The local bourgeoisie, clinging to medieval traditions, resisted military presence that could have revitalized the economy, reflecting broader regional trends seen in towns like Bourges.Issoudun's topography features a canal and fertile meadows, but the social fabric has deteriorated, lacking the class distinctions that once enriched community life. The absence of nobility and military presence has led to a homogenous society, with a local government of familiar faces becoming ineffective, further entrenching the town's inertia.In the early 19th century, young men in Issoudun formed the "Knights of Idleness," led by Maxence Gilet, a charismatic figure with a tumultuous past. The group engaged in mischievous pranks that terrorized the town, with Max endearing himself to the common folk despite bourgeois contempt. Under Max's leadership, the Knights executed elaborate pranks, often involving townspeople in humorous chaos, leading to the resignation of the sub-prefect due to relentless harassment. This chapter illustrates the interplay between social stagnation, youthful rebellion, and the decline of a once-thriving community, highlighting how the past influences Issoudun's present dynamics.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################As Joseph and Madame Bridau traveled from Orleans to Issoudun, the Knights of Idleness played a prank on Pere Fario by hoisting his cart onto the embankment, then returned to Mere Cognette’s for food and laughter, eager to see Fario's reaction.The Knights enjoyed leisure activities, with Max particularly admired by Madame Hochon's grandsons, Francois and Baruch, who relied on him for financial support. During a gathering, Baruch informed Max that Madame Bridau and her son were coming to Issoudun, which could threaten Max's interests in the property of Jean-Jacques Rouget, an old bachelor connected to Flore Brazier, Rouget's servant-mistress. Although Max initially dismissed the concern, he felt unsettled, knowing Rouget's wealth was tied to Flore, and the arrival of the legitimate heirs could jeopardize his plans.Francois and another friend speculated that if Rouget revoked his will in favor of Flore, it could impact Max's future. However, Max expressed confidence in his relationship with Flore and proposed that the Knights assist him in dealing with the Bridaus, clarifying that he did not intend to harm them but wanted to secure his position. The Knights pledged their support, toasting to Flore and dismissing the Bridaus, reflecting their relaxed morals. Max aimed to cultivate their loyalty for future support in reclaiming his status.As the night ended, Max contemplated the implications of the Bridaus' arrival. He realized Flore had not informed him about her family, which could threaten their financial future. His affection for Flore was intertwined with her potential inheritance from Rouget, and he felt an urgency to act before the Bridaus could secure their claim.The narrative shifts to Flore's backstory. Discovered by Doctor Rouget as a child, she was taken in due to her beauty and innocence, receiving a better life than she would have had with her uncle. Under Rouget's care, Flore enjoyed education and luxuries that distinguished her from other girls in Issoudun. Despite gossip surrounding their relationship, Flore thrived, though townspeople often misinterpreted Rouget's intentions. Her life changed dramatically when Rouget, nearing death, refused to leave her any inheritance, believing her beauty would ensure her future wealth.Jean-Jacques Rouget, at thirty-seven, was timid and socially awkward, particularly around women. He did not mourn his father as Flore did, having gained materially from him. His father had hoped to marry him off but hesitated, fearing a wife's influence. Instead, he instilled avarice in Jean-Jacques, who secretly loved Flore, the only woman in his life.Flore began to see Jean-Jacques differently after noticing his silence. Over three weeks, their relationship evolved, culminating in a conversation where he expressed his desire for her to stay. Intrigued by his affection, Flore found him less unattractive. Their dynamic shifted as Jean-Jacques, emboldened by his feelings, offered her a place in his life, leading to mutual understanding.As time passed, Flore took charge of the household, surpassing Jean-Jacques in management and influence. She became indispensable, and their lives settled into a routine, with Jean-Jacques growing happier under her care. However, Flore's beauty attracted attention, and she eventually fell for Maxence Gilet, a penniless officer, straining her relationship with Jean-Jacques.Flore's passion for Max led to tension, as she expressed frustrations with Jean-Jacques's perceived stinginess and argued for Max to move in. Despite his reluctance, Jean-Jacques agreed, hoping to maintain her affection. Once Max moved in, dynamics shifted again. Flore adopted a more refined lifestyle, complicating the interplay of love, power, and dependency among the three. Jean-Jacques found companionship in Max, while Flore reveled in her newfound status and influence.When Madame Bridau returned to Issoudun to safeguard her inheritance, Jean-Jacques Rouget had lapsed into a semi-vegetative state, motivated solely by his affection for Flore. Max, Rouget's companion, had adorned his apartment with provincial luxuries. One morning, Flore found Max asleep after a late night and learned that Rouget's family was coming to claim their inheritance. Concerned, Max advised Flore to act as if she hadn’t heard the news.A commotion erupted when Fario reported his cart missing. Max feigned indifference but soon gathered a crowd, humorously suggesting the cart had magically ascended the tower, leading to laughter at Fario's expense. Eventually, Max and his friends destroyed the cart, further enraging Fario and complicating Max's situation with Flore.As tensions rose, Francois informed Max of the Bridaus' imminent arrival. Max opted for manipulation rather than open opposition. Meanwhile, Flore, upset by the news, took her frustration out on Rouget, withholding affection and leaving him confused. Flore's harsh treatment stemmed from her fear of losing her position. She berated Rouget about his sister's visit, claiming it would disrupt their lives. Rouget, bewildered, insisted he knew nothing of their arrival.Max advised Rouget to welcome his family to avoid scandal, emphasizing the importance of appearances. Flore, recognizing Max's authority, softened and helped prepare breakfast, while Rouget expressed gratitude for Max's support. After breakfast, Max and Flore discussed financial strategies to secure Rouget's wealth, plotting to ensure the Bridaus would not interfere with their plans. Meanwhile, the Hochon household prepared for the Bridaus' arrival, with Madame Hochon urging her husband to treat the visitors kindly, while Hochon remained skeptical about their intentions. As the day progressed, news of the Bridaus' arrival divided the town, escalating tensions over Rouget's inheritance and shifting power dynamics within the community.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Agathe and Joseph arrived at the Messageries-Royales in Issoudun, where Agathe felt nostalgic. News of their arrival spread quickly, and Madame Hochon welcomed Agathe warmly, having developed a maternal affection for her. The family gathered in the salon for introductions, scrutinizing Joseph, whose appearance sparked unflattering comments about his health and character.As they settled in, Joseph noted the stark contrast between his artistic life in Paris and the austere provincial lifestyle. Dinner revealed the Hochons' frugality, which Joseph found disheartening. Despite the meager meal, Madame Hochon expressed her affection for Joseph and her hopes for his success as an artist. The evening was filled with gossip about local elites, particularly regarding Maxence Gilet and the Rabouilleuse's influence over Joseph's uncle, Jean-Jacques Rouget.After dinner, the Hochons and the Bridau family discussed the challenges of reclaiming the Rouget property from Max and Flore. Joseph expressed skepticism about their chances against such formidable opposition. The conversation shifted to the upcoming visit to Rouget, where Joseph was encouraged to make a good impression.The next morning, Joseph spotted his uncle outside, now unrecognizable and under Flore's influence. Agathe was distressed, while Joseph remarked on Flore's beauty despite her role in their family's troubles. The Hochons advised Joseph to visit Rouget alone to discuss family matters without Flore's interference.A letter from Rouget invited Joseph to dinner, eliciting mixed feelings from the Hochons, who suspected Flore's manipulations. They crafted a response emphasizing Agathe's struggles and Joseph's artistic aspirations, hoping to secure a favorable position in the family dynamics.As preparations for dinner unfolded, Gritte, the servant, reported on Flore's extravagant display, indicating her desire to impress. The Hochons recognized the need for caution, understanding the complexities of the situation with Max and Flore. Joseph was urged to remain observant and strategic during his visit, as the stakes were high in their quest to reclaim the family legacy.At four o’clock, Joseph arrived at Rouget's house, where he met Kouski, Flore, and Maxence Gilet. As a painter, Joseph admired Flore, which pleased Rouget. He and Max exchanged pleasantries about their fashionable appearances.Flore suggested a walk to see the tower of Issoudun before dinner, and Joseph eagerly agreed. While Flore prepared, Joseph admired the significant paintings in Rouget's home. Gilet mentioned more paintings in the attic, prompting Joseph to explore. Upon returning, Joseph was thrilled about the art, and Flore encouraged Rouget to give the paintings to Joseph, suggesting he could make copies for a fee. Rouget agreed to pay Joseph four thousand francs for the copies, much to Joseph's surprise and delight.News of Joseph's acquisition spread quickly through Issoudun, enhancing Flore's status. Dinner that evening was lavish, attended by local dignitaries, and Joseph left feeling tipsy and satisfied. Meanwhile, Rouget was carried to bed after indulging heavily in wine.Max, alone with Flore, discussed their strategy to keep the Bridaus at bay while benefiting from Rouget's wealth. They planned to send the paintings to Joseph while redecorating Rouget's home to distract him. The next day, Joseph awoke to find the paintings had been moved, and his mother, Agathe, visited Rouget to express gratitude for Flore's care.Old Hochon advised Agathe to cultivate a relationship with Flore to gain influence over Rouget. Despite her efforts, Agathe struggled to speak privately with her brother, now under Flore's influence. Meanwhile, the Knights of Idleness plotted mischief, including releasing rats into Fario's grain store, causing him significant losses.As the Bridaus struggled to gain Rouget's favor, Joseph received letters from painter Schinner and lawyer Desroches. Schinner invited him to work on a project, while Desroches warned Agathe about Maxence's manipulations, hinting that Rouget might still be swayed by remorse.Frustrated, Joseph decided to return to Paris. He bid farewell to Rouget, who seemed oblivious to the value of his paintings. Feeling insulted by Flore and Max's manipulation, Joseph returned the paintings to Rouget, asserting his independence as an artist.The incident quickly became gossip in Issoudun, with exaggerated tales about Joseph's departure. Old Hochon warned Agathe that Joseph's actions might jeopardize their chances of inheriting from Rouget, as Max and Flore were aware of the paintings' true worth. The chapter ends with uncertainty about the Bridaus' future and growing tension with Maxence and Flore.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Between midnight and one o’clock, the Knights of Idleness fed the town's dogs, finishing around three in the morning. Max was ambushed by Fario, who stabbed him before fleeing. Max's cries attracted Lousteau-Prangin and Goddet, but the townspeople ignored the commotion, accustomed to false alarms. After fainting, Max claimed to recognize Fario, prompting Lousteau-Prangin to inform his father, the judge.Max was taken home, where Goddet assessed the knife wound as serious but not fatal. Seizing the moment, Max plotted to frame Joseph Bridau, the painter, for the attack. He instructed Flore to spread rumors of his impending death, hoping this would lead to Joseph's arrest and force his mother to flee to Paris.Flore, feigning distress, accused Joseph of wanting Max dead to inherit from his uncle. The townspeople, stirred by her claims, began searching for Joseph, who had left Hochon’s house at dawn after a restless night. Alarmed by the crowd's anger, Hochon bolted his door, fearing for his gold. Meanwhile, Agathe, Joseph's mother, fainted upon hearing the news.As the crowd grew, the police struggled to maintain order. Gendarmes arrived just in time to protect Joseph from the mob, who were convinced of his guilt. Despite his protests of innocence, Joseph was taken to the Palais for his safety. He recounted his whereabouts that morning, providing an alibi that could be verified by witnesses.Initially skeptical, the magistrates agreed to investigate further but decided to detain Joseph for protection. He wrote a reassuring letter to his mother, calming her fears. Meanwhile, Max, recovering from his wound, plotted to keep Joseph imprisoned, believing it would rid them of the Parisians.As the investigation progressed, Max's deception unraveled. Witnesses confirmed Joseph's alibi, and Max's own letter to the judge, detailing the assailant's shoes, further implicated him. Eventually, Joseph was released, warmly greeted by his relieved mother.In the aftermath, the townspeople celebrated the departure of the Parisians as a victory. Joseph and Agathe returned to Paris, where Joseph's spirits lifted despite his brother Philippe's ongoing trial for actions during the political upheaval. Desroches, a lawyer, sought to help Philippe by arranging for his release and advising caution with Max, who was clever and dangerous. Ultimately, Philippe was released and prepared to confront Max and reclaim his family's legacy.On November 2nd, All-Souls’ Day, Philippe Bridau returned to Issoudun, registering with the local police and lodging on rue l’Avenier. His return as a banished officer linked to a military conspiracy stirred excitement, especially since he was the brother of a wrongfully accused painter. Maxence Gilet, now recovered, had converted Pere Rouget’s mortgages into cash, raising concerns in town. Monsieur Hochon sought information from notary Monsieur Heron about Gilet's actions, worried for the Bridau family.Gilet and Flore Brazier felt secure with Philippe's arrival, knowing they could manipulate old Rouget for his wealth. Philippe visited his uncle in worn clothing, reflecting his hardships, yet he maintained dignity, refusing financial help from Rouget due to past grievances. Flore, sensing Philippe's hostility, advised Rouget to avoid him.Philippe later visited the Hochons, introducing himself and expressing his intent to live modestly on his limited income. He engaged in polite conversation, winning over Madame Hochon by discussing her nephew, journalist Lousteau. Aware of the town's gossip, Philippe suggested a private discussion with Hochon to avoid suspicion, aiming to connect with other officers of the old army who opposed Gilet.Philippe soon acquired a new wardrobe on credit and befriended Mignonnet and Carpentier, fellow officers disapproving of Gilet. He shared insights about the failed conspiracy to overthrow the Bourbons, earning respect among the officers while plotting to undermine Gilet and secure his uncle's fortune.As Philippe ingratiated himself with the local elite, he maintained a facade of respectability, contrasting with Gilet's dubious reputation. He frequently dined with Mignonnet and Carpentier, solidifying his community position. Meanwhile, Gilet viewed Philippe as a rival.Philippe discovered that Gilet's associates, including Rouget's grandsons, were involved in dubious activities, which he reported to Hochon. This revelation heightened tensions, as Gilet and Flore plotted to secure Rouget's wealth. Philippe confronted his uncle about Flore's influence, insisting on a private discussion.After a tense exchange, Philippe met Hochon, who informed him that Rouget had not signed any power of attorney, preventing Gilet from accessing his funds. Philippe planned to monitor his uncle and Gilet closely, determined to protect his family's interests. Alarmed by Philippe's boldness, Flore relayed the details to Gilet, who proposed a risky plan to manipulate Rouget into signing a power of attorney, setting the stage for a confrontation over Rouget's fortune.As Philippe walked with his uncle Rouget along the boulevard Baron, he contemplated the impending clash between Colonel Bridau and Captain Gilet, both vying for Rouget's affections and fortune. Philippe expressed disdain for Gilet, revealing his plan to eliminate him to ensure Rouget's happiness with Flore, the woman they both loved. Despite Rouget's infatuation with Flore, Philippe urged him not to sign a power of attorney that would grant Gilet access to his wealth, warning that it was a scheme for Gilet and Flore to marry and leave Rouget destitute.Rouget, easily swayed by Flore's charms, proved difficult for Philippe to convince. Philippe insisted he would deal with Gilet if necessary, promising to make Flore obedient and devoted to Rouget. Their conversation shifted when they encountered Mignonnet and Carpentier, who were aware of Rouget's precarious situation. Philippe concluded that either Gilet must die or he would have to kill Gilet himself to save his uncle.Meanwhile, Gilet recognized the growing animosity against him in Issoudun due to his reckless behavior. He met with Captain Potel, who urged him to confront Bridau to restore his honor. However, Gilet was preoccupied with securing Rouget's wealth for himself and Flore, planning to transfer the funds to her name before any confrontation.At Rouget's home, Kouski, under Gilet's orders, feigned sorrow to inform Rouget that Flore had left with Vedie. This devastated Rouget, who searched for Flore in vain. Gilet attempted to maintain his reputation while plotting to keep Rouget under his control.As rumors of Gilet's misdeeds spread, the townspeople began to turn against him. Potel expressed dismay over the division within the Imperial Guard, now pitted against itself due to Gilet's actions. Feeling cornered, Gilet resolved to confront Bridau, believing he could outmaneuver him in a duel.Philippe, anticipating Gilet's next move, prepared to counteract his plans, suspecting Gilet would further manipulate Flore and Rouget. When Rouget returned home, he began drafting a letter to Flore, threatening to revoke his will in her favor if she did not return immediately.Seeing an opportunity, Philippe convinced Rouget to send the letter with Carpentier for personal delivery to Flore, believing this would bring her back and restore Rouget's authority.As evening approached, Philippe strategized to ensure Flore's compliance and Rouget's happiness. He planned to take Gilet's place in Rouget's life, promising to keep Flore in line. When Flore returned, Philippe confronted her, asserting his dominance and warning her of the consequences if she did not comply with Rouget's needs.The next day, gossip about the impending duel between Philippe and Gilet spread throughout Issoudun, with many speculating on the outcome. Flore, realizing the stakes, began to act more affectionately towards Rouget to secure her position. However, Gilet remained defiant, unwilling to back down from the challenge posed by Philippe. As tensions escalated, the townspeople rallied around Philippe, hoping for a resolution that would restore order and honor to the Imperial Guard, setting the stage for a dramatic confrontation that would determine their fates.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################The day after the coronation banquet, officers of the old army met at Lacroix's restaurant in Issoudun to discuss Max Gilet's dismissal from old Rouget's house. Few defended Max, and tensions escalated when he arrived, receiving a cold reception compared to Colonel Philippe Bridau. During the banquet, Philippe and Max exchanged barbs, aware of their upcoming duel. Philippe proposed a toast to the Grand Army, which was met with enthusiasm, while Max feigned joviality. After dessert, the duel was confirmed for the next morning, with sabres as weapons. Philippe was visited by Monsieur and Madame Hochon, who urged him to pray and offered a relic for protection. Despite skepticism, Philippe accepted it, feeling hopeful.The next morning, Max arrived at the duel site with his seconds, where Philippe awaited him. Both men displayed calmness as they prepared to fight. The duel began with a tense standoff, but Philippe quickly gained the upper hand, disarming Max. In a desperate move, Max attacked fiercely, resulting in both sustaining serious injuries. Ultimately, Philippe delivered a fatal blow to Max, ending the duel.Max's death marked the end of his ambitions and the influence of the Order of Idleness in Issoudun. As Philippe recovered, he wrote letters detailing his situation and plans regarding Flore Brazier, Max's former lover. He aimed to secure his inheritance and manipulate circumstances surrounding Flore, who fell ill after Max's death.Philippe devised a plan to marry Flore, believing it would solidify his position and inheritance. He encouraged his mother, Agathe, to support this union, framing it as a means to restore Flore's respectability. Eventually, Flore agreed to marry Rouget, influenced by Philippe's manipulations. The marriage contract was signed, securing Flore a substantial dowry and life annuity. Agathe, pleased with her son's apparent transformation, remained unaware of his true intentions, as Philippe's ambition allowed him to solidify control over Rouget's estate.Lieutenant-Colonel Philippe Bridau returned to Paris with his aunt and the ailing Rouget, facilitating the transfer of Rouget's income to himself. Philippe indulged them in a lavish lifestyle filled with actresses and artists, leading to Rouget's demise after a night out, attributed to indigestion. Philippe's aunt, Madame Rouget, was monitored by Mariette to prevent excessive indulgence.In October 1823, Philippe returned to Issoudun to settle his uncle's estate, netting 1.6 million francs, which he invested with Mongenod and Sons. In March 1824, he married Madame Rouget, securing her fortune in case of her death without children. He purchased a furnished apartment for her and a grand hotel on rue de Clichy, investing heavily in renovations and art.With Charles X's accession, Philippe gained favor and was appointed lieutenant-colonel in the Duc de Maufrigneuse's regiment. He leveraged his connections to secure a noble title, becoming Comte de Brambourg, and sought to marry Mademoiselle Amelie de Soulanges. His ambition led him to request ennoblement, which was granted due to his social standing.By January 1827, Philippe had risen to the Royal Guard and sought further honors, including a position as aide-de-camp to the Dauphin. He distanced himself from his past, refusing to associate with old friends who might tarnish his reputation. Philippe aspired to marry into high society and become a general.Meanwhile, Joseph Bridau struggled as an artist, supported by his mother Agathe, who managed a lottery office. Despite Joseph's talent, he faced financial difficulties and criticism, overshadowed by Philippe's success. Agathe, proud of Philippe's status, remained unaware of Joseph's struggles.Agathe eventually wrote to Philippe for financial help for Joseph but received a cold response that devastated her. This led to her declining health, and she sought solace from her confessor, realizing her misplaced affections for Philippe over Joseph. As she lay dying, she expressed regret for not loving Joseph as he deserved.Joseph, heartbroken by his mother's condition, refrained from mentioning Philippe during her illness. Agathe's death left him alone, and he wrote a bitter letter to Philippe, blaming him for their mother's demise. Philippe, feeling the weight of his actions, distanced himself further from the family.After Agathe's funeral, Joseph learned of Philippe's indifference and the dire state of his sister-in-law, Flore, who was suffering from neglect and illness. Joseph, along with friends, sought to help Flore, uncovering the extent of Philippe's cruelty towards her. They arranged for her care in a hospital, but she ultimately succumbed to her hardships.Philippe, now wealthy, faced societal scrutiny and rumors about his past. His ambition led to financial ruin during the July Revolution of 1830, where he lost most of his fortune. He returned to military service, hoping for glory but met a tragic end in battle in 1839.Joseph, having married well, inherited Philippe's estate and continued to paint, gaining recognition but never achieving the fame he desired. Despite his struggles, he found solace in his art and friendships, ultimately becoming Comte de Brambourg, a title he found ironic given his brother's legacy.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################**The Illustrious Gaudissart: Summary****Dedication**To Madame la Duchesse de Castries.**Chapter I**Felix Gaudissart, a successful commercial traveler in Parisian hats, represents the shift from material enterprise to intellectual pursuits following the political upheaval of 1830. He adapts to a new economy focused on intellectual property by engaging in life insurance and journalism to satisfy the public's demand for fresh ideas.**Chapter II**While training with a life-insurance company and writing for two newspapers, Gaudissart, driven by ambition, becomes enamored with a florist named Jenny, promising to furnish her room with his profits and boasting of market domination.**Chapter III**In Vouvray, Gaudissart encounters a local mindset resistant to new ideas. His subscription sales efforts are met with indifference, and he meets Monsieur Vernier, who initially appears supportive but ultimately mocks him. Vernier introduces him to Margaritis, a local lunatic who believes he is a retired banker. Gaudissart, unaware of Margaritis's condition, engages him in discussions about insurance, only to be outwitted.**Chapter IV**Margaritis's eccentricities lead to humorous exchanges, and despite his frustration, Gaudissart seeks his support. The situation escalates into a duel challenge, which becomes a farce, underscoring the absurdity of their conflict.**Chapter V**On duel day, both men recognize the ridiculousness of their feud and reconcile over breakfast. Gaudissart learns humility, realizing that his Parisian charm does not guarantee success in the provinces. He departs Vouvray with a deeper understanding of human nature and the challenges of selling ideas in a resistant environment, reflecting Balzac's broader themes of commerce and society.**Addendum**Characters from "The Illustrious Gaudissart" appear in other Balzac works, connecting this story to his larger literary universe, the Human Comedy.######################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################**DEDICATION**  To Monsieur le Comte Ferdinand de Gramont, whose contributions to life and heraldry studies have enriched my work. I value our friendship.**THE MUSE OF THE DEPARTMENT**  Sancerre, a picturesque town in Le Berry along the Loire, is known for its fertile lands and wine production, strategically located near the river-port of Saint-Thibault, enhancing trade, particularly in wine, often compared to Burgundy's finest. However, Sancerre is in decline as trade shifts to the right bank of the Loire, with neighboring Cosne gaining prominence. With about 3,500 residents, Sancerre retains its charm, featuring narrow streets and remnants of its feudal past, including a citadel that survived a fierce siege during the religious wars.The town is home to notable figures like medical pioneer Horace Bianchon and journalist Etienne Lousteau. Yet, Sancerre struggles under the influence of powerful landowners, leading to a failed attempt to escape their electoral control. This conspiracy highlights the challenges of provincial politics, where true leadership is rare. The community's desire for change is evident in plans to support a local champion for the next election, inspired by a prominent woman in the borough.In the 1830s, Sancerre was notable for the Superior Woman, who gained local fame alongside Lousteau and Bianchon. George Sand's influence led to the emergence of _Sandism_, recognizing many overlooked superior women in France. The Superior Woman of Sancerre resided at La Baudraye, linked to the Milaud family, whose lineage included a sheriff converted from Calvinism during Louis XIV's reign.Jean Athanase Polydore Milaud de la Baudraye inherited a diminished estate and struggled with health issues. In 1800, he returned to Sancerre to restore his family's legacy, marrying Dinah Piedefer, a beautiful and intelligent woman from a modest background. Despite skepticism about their marriage, Dinah transformed La Baudraye into a stylish residence, enhancing her social standing. However, her superiority alienated her from other women, reflecting the challenges faced by women aspiring to rise above societal expectations in 19th-century France.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################By the end of 1825, Dinah de la Baudraye faced accusations of favoring male visitors, which tarnished her reputation. Despite her attempts to maintain a respectable image, she boldly stated to a false friend, “I would rather have my bowl empty than with anything in it!” This remark backfired, especially as she remained childless after five years of marriage, making her a target of ridicule.Monsieur de la Baudraye, her husband, was frail and often mocked for his appearance. He wore false calves to appear taller and was gentle, rarely contradicting Dinah. In contrast, Dinah was admired for her beauty and intelligence, engaging in conversations that showcased her extensive knowledge, leading to comparisons with Madame de Staël.Dinah's charm allowed her to thrive as a "Superior Woman." She immersed herself in the arts, promoting the romantic school and collecting antiques at their home, La Baudraye. Her collection fascinated visitors, who were captivated by her knowledge of art and history.To cultivate a literary circle in Sancerre, Dinah partnered with local judge Monsieur Boirouge, who had ulterior motives. However, the group devolved into social gatherings filled with games and drinks, disappointing Dinah’s aspirations for intellectual discourse. Despite her efforts, the members often strayed into frivolity, leaving her disheartened.Dinah's admirers, including three prominent men, vied for her attention, believing she would eventually become more accessible. However, she remained devoted to her ideals. Monsieur de la Baudraye recognized his leverage as her husband and exploited her admirers for personal gain, securing favorable outcomes in legal matters. Meanwhile, local women speculated about the nature of Dinah's marriage, suspecting hidden secrets, while a slow domestic drama unfolded within La Baudraye. Throughout her early married life, Dinah maintained her virtue amidst societal expectations and personal ambitions.Mademoiselle Dinah Piedefer, whose academic success was fueled by pride, married Monsieur de la Baudraye, a seemingly suitable match despite their age difference. Initially, he allowed her to improve their home but soon revealed his miserly nature, restricting her personal expenses. Dinah's attempts to manipulate him through jealousy failed, as he remained indifferent to her emotional needs.As time passed, Dinah grew frustrated with her provincial life in Sancerre, feeling trapped and yearning for the excitement of Paris. She had hoped her husband would rise socially, but he remained stagnant, leaving her humiliated and unfulfilled. Despite her ambitions, she found herself surrounded by mediocrity, dulling her intellect and spirit.Dinah's six years in Sancerre transformed her into a typical provincial woman, eroding her individuality. The lack of stimulating company stifled her creativity, and she became aware of her decline when comparing herself to her friend Anna, who thrived in Parisian society. This realization plunged her into despair, recognizing that while Anna lived fully, she merely endured.In her frustration, Dinah turned to poetry, producing "Paquita la Sevillane," which garnered attention but exposed her vulnerabilities. The poem reflected her inner turmoil and longing for a vibrant life, contrasting sharply with her dull existence. Despite its success, she feared backlash from her peers if her authorship were revealed.After the Revolution of 1830, George Sand's fame inspired a surge in aspiring women writers, including Dinah, who gained attention under the pseudonym Jan Diaz. Despite initial obscurity, her works garnered public sympathy after her untimely death.Monsieur de Clagny published a collection of Jan Diaz's works, which received mixed reviews. As Dinah's reputation grew, she emerged as a rival to George Sand, with her poetry receiving newfound praise. The local community celebrated her as a "Superior Woman," a title that replaced her earlier nickname, "The Sappho of Saint-Satur."Dinah's marriage was strained as her literary ambitions clashed with domestic life. Her spiritual advisor, Abbe Duret, warned her about her husband's character, but she struggled to accept his insights. Seeking solace in writing, Dinah's frustrations led her to contemplate infidelity.Despite her initial resolve to stop writing after a confrontation with her husband, Dinah felt increasingly isolated. The local society clamored for more of her poetry, unaware of her personal turmoil. Her husband's passive demeanor deepened her despair.As Dinah navigated her complex emotions, she became enamored with Monsieur de Clagny, believing his support indicated love. This led to a political campaign to elect him as a deputy, but jealousy and rivalry undermined their efforts, resulting in disappointment.Yearning for freedom from her oppressive marriage, Dinah reflected on her unfulfilled dreams and societal constraints stifling her creativity. The arrival of Parisian literary figures offered her a glimpse of a world beyond her provincial existence, revealing her sharp wit and desire for intellectual engagement. Ultimately, Dinah's journey illustrates the struggles of a woman caught between societal expectations and artistic ambitions in a patriarchal society.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################At five in the morning, guests at the Chateau d’Anzy were awake, with La Baudraye eager to showcase his land reclamation project to the Parisians. He planned a day of sport to impress them with the twelve hundred acres he aimed to reclaim for profit.Gatien Boirouge noted the absence of the Public Prosecutor, who was in court, raising speculation about Madame de la Baudraye's feelings for Monsieur de Clagny. Gatien worried she might be drawn to the Prosecutor's ambitions, but Lousteau suggested they focus on enjoying the day.During the hunt, conversation shifted to love and fidelity. Lousteau proposed to provoke reactions from Madame de la Baudraye and de Clagny with scandalous stories. Bianchon defended Dinah’s innocence, while Gatien expressed concern about her being alone with Clagny. They agreed to maintain a diplomatic demeanor while observing the couple.Later, Lousteau recited a poem, which received mixed reactions. The evening included discussions on literature and morality, where Lousteau critiqued societal hypocrisy regarding love. They shared stories, including one about a young man imprisoned for political reasons who escaped, highlighting themes of betrayal and revenge.As the night progressed, tension grew between the Parisians and locals. Lousteau's sharp wit contrasted with the conservative views of others.
